                                            City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
                 Our Vision for the
                                                             Future
   Vision Statement
Our Vision Statement expresses our shared ideals and desires for the future of
Oakdale, and establishes the foundation for the 2030 General Plan. This vision
evolved through community and decision maker input as an integral component of
the General Plan Update process. The 2030 General Plan builds upon the existing
strengths and values of our community, while balancing new opportunities,
challenges, and initiatives. The 2030 General Plan recognizes that change must be
managed carefully to sustain our community’s quality of life, economic vitality,
social well-being, and environmental resources.
Beyond the 2030 General Plan, our vision is intended to influence all activities
which shape the future of our community. Our success will ultimately be measured
by our ability to achieve our vision, and the spirit by which we do so.
Our vision for Oakdale in the future includes…
                                                           PREFACE               i

  City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
   A SMALL TOWN CHARACTER AND SENSE OF COMMUNITY
   We are a distinct family-oriented community with a prosperous small town
   character that defines our strong sense of place. We have successfully balanced
   tradition and progress, embracing our unique heritage. We are enriched by the
   diversity of our residents, including seniors and youth, and are dedicated to
   protecting and enhancing our quality of life. We have a high level of community
   pride, and a shared sense of responsibility for our well-being.
   A VITAL AND DISTINCT DOWNTOWN
   Our historic downtown serves as the civic, social, and symbolic center of our
   community. It has been enhanced through the revitalization and reuse of historic
   storefronts, and the introduction of new buildings and parking designed to fit in
   with its architectural character. It is a walkable destination, with a wide variety of
   mixed-use, residential, shopping, restaurant, and entertainment options. Small
   businesses are encouraged, and community events are common.
   WELL PLANNED AND MANAGED GROWTH
   We have maintained a careful growth strategy promoting development that is
   beneficial to both our economy and small town quality of life. Growth and
   revitalization areas are clearly defined and surrounding rural lands protected.
   Growth is located where appropriate service levels can be provided, and is
   required to pay its own way. We emphasize high-quality, fiscally sustainable
   development and a compact community form that incorporates smart growth
   principles.
   AN ATTRACTIVE COMMUNITY
   We have a positive visual image and an attractive community. Our buildings,
   public spaces, and streetscapes are well designed and landscaped. We have
   beautified and created visually distinguishable districts along our primary
   transportation corridors, Yosemite Avenue and F Street. We have high design
   expectations, but implement those expectations with sensitivity and flexibility.
ii                  PREFACE
                                             City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
DIVERSE RESIDENTIAL NEIGHBORHOODS
We have a variety of distinct, well maintained, residential neighborhoods. Our
neighborhoods provide for a diversity of densities and quality housing choices to
accommodate residents at all income levels and stages of life. Neighborhoods
have tree-lined streets, are interconnected and walkable, and are integrated with
parks, open space, and schools. Residential growth has been balanced with job
growth.
RETAIL, SERVICE, AND ENTERTAINMENT CHOICES
We have been successful in attracting a variety of quality retail shops, restaurants,
and unique local services. Theater and other cultural and entertainment venues
thrive. Neighborhood retail and services exist throughout the City close to where
people live, including the previously underserved west side of town. Sales tax
leakage to other communities has been reduced, and we have capitalized on
pass through tourism services. We support a local business-friendly environment.
BROAD OPPORTUNITIES FOR INDUSTRY AND EMPLOYMENT
We have diverse employment opportunities allowing residents to work in the
community, and reducing commutes to distant jobs. We have successfully
retained existing industries and built upon our historic job base to attract new
opportunities. We have taken advantage of our assets including the adjacent
agricultural areas, easy railroad access, connectivity to the North County
Corridor, and nearby airport. We support vocational training and education to
enhance and expand the skills of the local workforce.
A SAFE COMMUNITY WITH QUALITY PUBLIC SERVICES
We are a safe community with reliable law enforcement and fire protection. We
have great hospital and medical services, schools, libraries, and community
centers. We have easy access to parks, trails, and recreational activities
promoting healthy lifestyles for all ages. The City provides cost-effective and
sustainable infrastructure and services.
                                                            PREFACE                   iii
  City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
   TRANSPORTATION OPTIONS
   Traffic flows efficiently through the community considering high levels of regional
   pass-through travel. Our roads accommodate multi-modal transportation options
   and connect to regional transportation improvements such as the North County
   Corridor. Bike and walking trails are conveniently located and linked to adjacent
   uses. Transit is accessible and reliable to all, including seniors and students. We
   have maximized opportunities to improve traffic flows as feasible and promote
   alternatives to the automobile.
   A GREEN COMMUNITY
   We have preserved significant habitat, open space, and scenic resources along
   the Stanislaus River corridor. This corridor is a community asset with public access,
   trails, and vistas with restaurants and entertainment options. We have maintained
   rural and agricultural lands around the City to provide separation from other
   communities and enhance our distinct identity. We are recognized as leaders in
   protecting air and water quality, resource conservation, and green practices.
   RESPONSIVE GOVERNANCE AND REGIONAL LEADERSHIP
   Our citizens are active and engaged, and our elected officials listen and respond
   to the interests of the community. The City is fiscally responsible working within
   available resources to provide for the cost effective delivery of services and
   allocating fair share costs to those who benefit. We profit from partnerships
   between government, business, service providers, developers, and citizens. We
   continue to face our regional responsibilities, and lead intergovernmental efforts to
   address our collective needs.
iv                  PREFACE
OAKDALE 2030 GENERAL PLAN
INTRODUCTION

                                               City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
                                          Introduction
The Oakdale 2030 General Plan (2030 General Plan or General Plan) presents a
vision for the City’s future and a strategy to make that vision a reality. It serves as a
long-term policy framework to guide the City’s growth and conservation in a
manner that sustains the community’s quality of life, economic vitality, social well-
being, and environmental resources. This document is the result of the collective
efforts of numerous stakeholders including elected and appointed officials, City staff,
public agencies, utility companies, local school districts, and the City residents who
have articulated their hopes, values and expectations for Oakdale’s future.
  What is a General Plan?
State law requires each city and county to prepare and adopt a comprehensive,
long-range general plan to guide its future. A general plan directs the physical
growth and conservation of resources within a jurisdiction. A general plan must
contain an integrated and internally consistent set of goals, policies, and
implementation programs that seek to achieve a community’s shared vision, and
address those issues of greatest importance to the City.
Much of a general plan’s content is established by statutory requirements. The legal
adequacy of a general plan is critical, since all development projects, subdivisions,
zoning regulations, specific plans, capital improvements, and other related plans,
regulations, and actions are required to be consistent with a community’s general
                                                     INTRODUCTION                        I-1

    City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
    plan. A general plan provides a jurisdiction with a consistent decision-making
    framework for determining whether specific land use and development proposals
    reflect the community’s physical, economic, social, and environmental goals. A
    general plan also presents guidance to decision-makers in establishing priorities for
    the allocation of resources, provision of public services, and initiation of public
    improvements.
    State law requires that general plans address seven topics, also referred to as
    “elements”. These required elements include land use, housing, open space,
    conservation, safety, circulation, and noise. A jurisdiction may also choose to
    include optional elements that address other topics of local interest. Once adopted,
    these optional elements carry the same legal weight as the required elements. Under
    State law, a jurisdiction may amend or update all or part of its general plan at a
    given time.
      General Plan
      Organization & Content
    OVERALL PLAN ORGANIZATION
    The City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan includes all information required in the
    seven state mandated elements. In some cases, the City has chosen to consolidate
    required information and/or reorganize content by common topics. In addition, the
    2030 General Plan includes optional elements that address economic vitality, public
    facilities, and community services.
I-2                   INTRODUCTION

                                                City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
The Oakdale 2030 General Plan is organized as follows:
               Introduction presents information on the General Plan’s organization,
               summarizes the planning context, and describes the General Plan
               update process.
               The Land Use & Community Form Element establishes the desired
               pattern of growth, revitalization and conservation, with specific
               direction on the type, location, extent, form and key design
               expectations for different land uses.
               The Economic Vitality Element includes goals and policies to enhance
               the economic well-being and sustainability of the City and to attract,
               grow and retain a stable and diverse range of businesses.
               The Mobility Element provides a framework that supports a balanced,
               multi-modal transportation network accommodating motor vehicles,
               bicyclists, pedestrians, public transit, rail and air travel.
               The Public Facilities Element establishes policies and standards for the
               planning and provision of water, wastewater, storm drainage, solid
               waste, energy and telecommunications services and infrastructure.
               The Community Services Element establishes requirements for the
               delivery of police and fire protection, parks and recreation,
               educational facilities, libraries and health care.
               The Natural Resources Element provides goals and policies to
               protect, enhance, and manage the City’s natural resources including
               open space and biological resources, agriculture, air quality, water
               resources and quality, minerals, and visual resources.
               The Public Health & Safety Element establishes goals and policies
               that minimize the risk of personal injury, loss of life, and property
               damage associated with flooding, geologic, and seismic hazards, as
               well as hazardous materials.
               The Noise Element provides policies and standards to control and
               minimize noise from transportation and stationary sources.
               Implementation summarizes the Standard Implementation Tools and
               Specific Implementation Programs required to carry out the goals and
               policies of the General Plan.
               Glossary of Terms & Acronyms defines terminology used in the 2030
               General Plan.
The Housing Element establishes the strategy to address the City of Oakdale’s
identified housing needs and quantified objectives. The Housing Element is required
by state law to be updated every eight years, necessitating a different review
process and implementation programs than the remainder of the General Plan. The
City’s Housing Element for the 2007– 2014 period was adopted by the City Council
and certified by the State Housing Community Development Department in advance

                                       City’s Housing Element is provided under separate cover (not bound with the
                                       remainder of the General Plan).

                                            INTRODUCTION                I-5

    City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
    RELATED DOCUMENTS
    Documents prepared in support of and as companion pieces to the Oakdale
    2030 General Plan include the Technical Background Report, Climate Action
    Plan, and Environmental Impact Report. These documents should be referred to
    for more extensive information on existing conditions, potential impacts and
    mitigation.
    Technical Background Report
    The Technical Background Report (TBR) provides a descriptive profile and analysis
    of existing conditions and trends for the City of Oakdale and its surrounding area
    (2030 General Plan Study Area) at the initiation of the General Plan update process
    (2009). The TBR presents physical, social and economic information that established
    the foundation for issue identification, policy development, and the setting for the
    2030 General Plan Environmental Impact Report.
    Climate Action Plan
    The Oakdale Climate Action Plan (CAP) serves to outline strategies, goals, and
    actions for reducing municipal and community-wide greenhouse gas (GHG)
    emissions. The CAP has been structured to ensure that the City does its part to meet
    the mandates of California’s Global Warming Solutions Act of 2006 (AB 32), while
    taking into account the City’s 2030 General Plan vision for future growth. AB 32
    requires jurisdictions to reduce greenhouse gas emissions to be at or below 1990
    levels by 2020.
    The CAP provides a roadmap for the City to be proactive in reducing GHGs
    through a series of local actions. Although the CAP is considered a “stand alone”
    policy document, the strategies presented in the CAP are incorporated into the
    2030 General Plan, where appropriate, and are consistent with the goals, policies,
    and implementation included in the General Plan.
    The CAP includes the following components:
        A GHG inventory for 2005 and for future forecasted years 2020 and 2030;
        A GHG reduction goal of 29% below business-as-usual emissions for 2020;
        Identification and transparent quantification of state-level GHG reduction
         strategies;
        Identification and transparent cost/benefit quantification of feasible reduction
         strategies and actions;
        Identification of implementation steps and financing mechanisms to achieve the
         GHG reduction goal;
        Procedures for monitoring and updating the GHG inventory and reduction
         measures every five years; and
        Identification of an implementation schedule and responsible parties for
         implementation.
I-6                    INTRODUCTION

                                               City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
Environmental Impact Report
A program environmental impact report (EIR) was prepared for the 2030 General
Plan, consistent with the requirements of the California Environmental Quality Act.
The EIR assesses the potential adverse environmental impacts from implementation
of the General Plan, identifies actions that will be undertaken to reduce or avoid
significant impacts where feasible, and evaluates the comparative impacts of a
range of alternatives. Some mitigation measures identified in the EIR are
incorporated as policies or implementation programs in the 2030 General Plan.
The EIR may support project-level environmental analysis for later projects
determined consistent with the General Plan, or the EIR findings may be used to
determine the appropriate level of environmental review that should be performed
when subsequent projects consistent with the General Plan are proposed.
   Planning Context
REGIONAL CONTEXT
The City of Oakdale is located at the base of the foothills of the Sierra Nevada
Mountains in eastern Stanislaus County, California, approximately 15 miles northeast
of Modesto, 20 miles north of Turlock, 22 miles east of Manteca, and 30 miles
southeast of Stockton. Other smaller cities surrounding Oakdale include Escalon (9
miles northwest), Riverbank (5 miles west), Waterford (10 miles southeast), Hughson
(13 miles south), and Ceres (18 miles southwest). Oakdale’s location is reflected on
Figure I-2, Regional Location.
The topography of Oakdale is generally flat, with rolling hills southeast and northwest
of the City limits. Historically the City and surrounding area have been used for
orchards and other agricultural operations. There are pockets of active agricultural
lands within the city limits, with a majority of the lands surrounding Oakdale still in
agricultural or rural uses. Some lands both within and outside of the City have
been designated as Prime Farmland, Farmland of Statewide Importance, and
Unique Farmland by the California Department of Conservation Farmland
Monitoring and Mapping Program (FMMP). Some of these properties are under
Williamson Act contracts (California Land Conservation Act of 1965).
The Stanislaus River winds through the northern edge of the City offering
recreational opportunities at designated parks and recreation areas. While outside
of the river corridor, much of the natural habitats in the City have been lost or
altered due to development and agricultural operations, scattered areas of
perennial grasslands, riparian woodlands, oak woodlands, and a variety of
wetlands including vernal pools, seasonal wetlands, freshwater marshes, ponds,
streams, and rivers still exist.

OAKDALE PROFILE
Development of the town of Oakdale began in 1871 when the Stockton and
Visalia Railroad (later absorbed by the Southern Pacific Railroad) established a
terminal in the area. With the arrival of the railroad, Oakdale was transformed
from a small agricultural settlement into a center for the transport of people and
goods to support mining and lumbering operations in the nearby Sierra
foothills. These businesses required the development of stables, warehouses, and
other freight-related businesses, which in turn led to the establishment of
merchandise stores, hotels, blacksmith shops, and other commercial enterprises
to support Oakdale’s growing population.
Much of Oakdale’s early residential growth was generated by testimonials that
the area boasted the healthiest weather and finest farming land in the valley.
These testimonials persuaded many individuals and families from nearby
Knights Ferry, Sonora, and Copperopolis to relocate to Oakdale, which was
named for the abundant Oak trees in the area. The growth of Oakdale is
believed to have resulted in the demise of the nearby town of Langworth, which
had been plotted as a town in 1860.
A second major impetus for growth in Oakdale was the formation of the
Oakdale Irrigation District in 1888. The District built a dam at Wildcat Creek,
two miles below present Tulloch Dam in Tuolumne County, which brought water
to the area. The establishment of a reliable water supply created a boom in
farming and ranching activity, with wheat, barley, and grains being the most
abundant crops. The agricultural boom resulted in substantial growth of the
residential population, as well as expansion of commercial and service-related
businesses to support the growing populace.
The steady growth that had characterized Oakdale since its inception
                                                    INTRODUCTION                   I-9

    City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
    encountered a setback in 1897 when the Sierra Railway extended its tracks to
    nearby Jamestown. Although many businesses related to the transport of goods,
    services, and people to the foothills closed or relocated to Jamestown, Oakdale
    continued to serve as a supply center for local agricultural operations.
    Oakdale was incorporated as a City in 1906. Four years later the Oakdale
    Irrigation District increased water supplied from the Stanislaus River, which
    enabled the development of numerous nut and fruit orchards, vineyards, and
    other lucrative crops that are important to Oakdale’s and the surrounding area’s
    economy to the present day.
    The development of industrialized food production in the early twentieth century
    brought about the largest period of growth in Oakdale’s history. The Pacific Pea
    Packing Plant was established in Oakdale in 1906, followed by numerous other
    processing and packing operations, which attracted a large workforce and
    resulted in an expansion of service industries and residential neighborhoods. By
    the mid-twentieth century, Oakdale’s economy and population were rooted
    firmly in the agricultural production, processing, and packing industries.
    Large areas of industrial land were annexed to the south of Oakdale beginning
    in 1965 and more recently with the 2006 South Oakdale Industrial Specific Plan
    area. Recent residential specific plan area annexations include the Burchell Hill
    Specific Plan area (1997), the Bridle Ridge Specific Plan area (1999), and the
    East F Street Specific Plan area (2006). At the time of adoption of the 2030
    General Plan, the City of Oakdale (City limits) was approximately 6.1 square
    miles. Annexation of the Crane Crossing and Sierra Pointe Specific Plans is
    anticipated shortly following adoption of the 2030 General Plan, further expanding
    the City.
    Over the years, Oakdale has experienced a healthy rate of growth, emerging
    as a distinct freestanding small urban community. This growth reflects the City’s
    desirable qualities as a livable community and its success in planning the pace,
    location, and character of new development. The first General Plan for the City
    of Oakdale was adopted on May 4, 1959. Numerous amendments and
    modifications have been made to the General Plan over the years. Prior to the
    current General Plan update, the last comprehensive update of the General Plan
    (2015 General Plan), was adopted in January 1994 with subsequent amendments
    though December 2003.
    Existing land uses within the City are generally characterized by established and
    newer suburban style residential neighborhoods, an historic Downtown,
    commercial corridors, a large industrial area, public/semi public uses, and
    parkland. Uses surrounding the City, including its future sphere of influence,
    consist primarily of agriculture with active field crops, orchards, dairy
    production, pastureland, and/or livestock grazing. Some of these areas also
    include large lot suburban or rural estate homes.
    F Street and Yosemite Avenue comprise the foundation of Oakdale’s transportation
    network and serve much of the City’s commute and regional traffic between the
    Sierra Nevada Mountains and the San Joaquin Valley. F Street and Yosemite
    Avenue north of F streets are designated as state highways (SR 108 and SR 120).
    Oakdale is often referred to as “The Gateway to Yosemite,” a reference to Yosemite
I-10                  INTRODUCTION

                                             City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
National Park and the Sierra Nevada Mountains accessible via SR 120 heading
east from the City. Due to the number of professional cowboys who have lived in
Oakdale and the annual Oakdale Rodeo established in 1954, the City has also
picked up the moniker of the "Cowboy Capital of the World."
Oakdale is served by four rail lines: the Burlington Northern and Santa Fe (BNSF)
Railroad, the Union Pacific (UP) Railroad, the Oakdale Traction Corporation (OTC),
and the Sierra Railroad. These lines extend east-west and north-south through the
City, primarily serving the movement of commerce. In addition, the Oakdale
Municipal Airport is located southeast of the City. The airport was originally
constructed in 1945, and has been owned and operated by the City of Oakdale
since 1960.
The City of Oakdale provides a range of quality public facilities and community
services. Within its corporate limits, the City provides water, wastewater, storm
drainage, police, fire, parks & recreation, and general administrative services. The
Oakdale Joint Unified School District (OJUSD) serves Oakdale residents and
portions of the surrounding area, and libraries are provided by Stanislaus County.
Solid waste, energy, and telecommunications are supplied by various outside
providers.
According to the 2010 census, the largest racial/ethnic groups in Oakdale are
White (68.1 percent) followed by Hispanic (26.1 percent). The median age for
Oakdale residents is 34.9 years, with approximately 27.9 percent of the
population under 18 years of age, and 12.3 percent over 65. Slightly over 89
percent of the City’s residents completed high school, with almost 38 percent
obtaining a college degree. Approximately 61.1 percent of the homes in the
City are owner occupied, and 38.9 percent occupied by renters. The medium
household income in 2010 was $59,842.
Oakdale’s current economy centers on agriculture, food manufacturing, and
tourism. The City is home to large food manufacturing operations including Ball
Western Can Company; ConAgra Foods, one of the largest tomato processing
plants in the world; and Sconza Candy Company. The top non-manufacturing
employers in the City are Oakdale Joint Unified School District, Oak Valley
Hospital, the City of Oakdale, and Burchell Nursery.
While the economy had significantly slowed at the time of this General Plan’s
adoption, Oakdale is projected to grow at a steady pace in the future. The
City’s population is estimated to increase from approximately 20,839 residents
in 2011 to 35,000 in 2030. This growth will result in the demand for
approximately 4,800 new residential units, a majority of which are anticipated
to be low and medium density residential. Employment in the City is estimated to
increase from 4,969 employees in 2011 to over 9,500 in 2030. This growth
will result in the need for approximately 2.7 million square feet of new non-
residential uses, a little more than 50 percent of which is anticipated to be
industrial, 20 percent public/semi-public and other similar uses, 15 percent
retail-oriented, with the remainder constituting various forms of office
development. (Goodwin Consulting Group, 2012).
The additional growth projected in Oakdale will provide both opportunities and
challenges to the City and its residents, resulting in the need to continue to
                                                    INTRODUCTION                    I-11

    City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
    thoughtfully manage growth and community character. The 2030 General Plan
    provides a policy framework to help the City maximize key opportunities and
    direct resources to improve its quality of life, provide new opportunities and
    foster a vibrant economy.
    PLANNING AREAS
    Under State law, a general plan must address the entire area within the
    jurisdiction’s boundaries and any lands outside its boundaries that bear relationship
    to its planning efforts. The planning boundaries considered as part of the 2030
    General Plan are illustrated on Figure I-3, and include:
        2030 Planning Area encompasses the area that is the focus of the General
         Plan, including the City limits and Oakdale’s existing and potential future
         spheres of influence. The General Plan designates land uses to all properties
         within the Planning Area. It is anticipated that the full Planning Area may
         ultimately be incorporated into the City to accommodate growth through 2030
         and beyond.
        Study Area encompasses a larger boundary extending beyond of the 2030
         Planning Area. The Study Area was considered as part of the General Plan
         Update, and background information was collected for the entirety of the area.
         County land uses apply to lands within the Study Area outside of the 2030
         Planning Area.
    For planning purposes, the General Plan establishes 2030 as the horizon year for
    buildout of the plan. It is recognized that the General Plan includes land use
    capacity that extends beyond 2030. Unless otherwise noted, goals and policies are
    structured to address ultimate buildout conditions.
    SPECIFIC PLAN AREAS
    Specific plans implement the general plan by applying planning policy that refine
    general plan policies applicable to the area and directly regulating land use.
    Oakdale has six adopted and four identified future specific plan areas located
    within and immediately adjacent to the City, as shown in Figure I-4. The six
    adopted specific plans include:
        Bridle Ridge Specific Plan: located south of Highway 108 along the western
         edge of the City, consisting primarily of low density residential homes, parks,
         schools, and commercial uses;

    Burchell Hill Specific Plan: located south of the Stanislaus River and east of
     Downtown Oakdale, consisting primarily of low density residential homes,
     parks, and schools;
    South Industrial Specific Plan: located in the southern portion of Oakdale,
     primarily planned for industrial uses;

     public/semi-public uses.
Four areas are designated as future specific plans. These include the Downtown
District, and Future Specific Plan Areas 2, 5, and 8, as shown in Figure I-4. These
future specific plan areas are intended to accommodate new growth and
revitalization during the 2030 General Plan horizon. It is envisioned that the
Downtown District will be redeveloped to include a broader mix of local and
regional serving retail, goods, services, offices, restaurants and entertainment, as
well as higher density housing and mixed use development. Future Specific Plan
Areas 2, 5, and 8 are envisioned to accommodate single- and multi-family
residential units along with commercial uses, parks and schools (see Land Use and
Community Form Element for additional detail).
                                                     INTRODUCTION                    I-15

    City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
      General Plan Update
    GENERAL PLAN UPDATE PROCESS
    The Oakdale 2030 General Plan represents the first comprehensive update of the
    City’s General Plan since 1994. Conditions and considerations within and outside
    the City have changed considerably since 1994. The 2030 General Plan responds
    to these changes to reflect present day issues, values, and compliance with evolving
    state laws. It also ensures that the Plan is based upon the latest data (e.g., growth
    projections and economic indicators) and up-to-date (GIS) mapping.
    The City of Oakdale began the process of developing a new General Plan in the
    latter half of 2009. The update process included the following key steps:
I-16                   INTRODUCTION

                                                 City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
COMMUNITY OUTREACH
A community outreach program was implemented as part of the General Plan
update process to solicit thoughts and ideas from the public regarding Oakdale’s
future. This input has directly shaped the City’s vision for the future and the 2030
General Plan. Components of the community outreach program included:
    Community Workshops: Three community workshops were held during the
     General Plan update process. At the first workshop, input was solicited on the
     community’s vision for the future and identification of key issues. At the second,
     participants were asked to formulate citywide land use alternatives, and identify
     specific areas of desired change. At the last workshop, participants were asked
     to help define policy direction for both the General Plan and Climate Action
     Plan.
    Public Meetings and Hearings: Several joint City Council and Planning
     Commission Study Sessions were held throughout the update process to provide
     input on major milestones and confirm the direction of the 2030 General Plan.
     Each of these study sessions were open to the public and served as an
     opportunity for the community to provide input.
    Landowner Requests: City staff solicited requests from interested landowners
     regarding potential land use changes. These requests were considered as part
     of the development of the preferred Land Use Plan.
    Notice of Preparation Scoping Meeting: A scoping meeting was held during
     the Notice of Preparation comment period for the EIR. Participants were asked
     to comment on the scope of the EIR.
    Stakeholder Interviews: Several interviews were held with key stakeholders
     selected by City staff to define their initial perspectives on the City’s key assets
     and issues. The stakeholders represented a range of community interests.
    Newsletters: General Plan newsletters were distributed in mailings to City
     residents during major project milestones to convey information on the progress
     of the General Plan update, and to identify upcoming opportunities to provide
     input.
    Website: A website dedicated to the 2030 General Plan was established to
     keep the community informed of progress, and to allow for the downloading of
     information and opportunity to provide comments at any time during the
     General Plan update process. (http://ci.oakdale.ca.us/gp/)
The input received during the General Plan update process significantly influenced
the overall shape and direction of the 2030 General Plan, helping to ensure that it
reflects the community’s values and aspirations for the future.

                                 LAND USE & COMMUNITY FORM                         LU-1

    City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
      Community Form
    The City of Oakdale is formed by a mix of established and newly developed
    suburban neighborhoods, substantial commercial and industrial districts, and
    undeveloped lands. The City’s growth in recent decades is reflective of its reputation
    as a livable community and its success in determining the pace and location of new
    development. Additional growth is projected for Oakdale through the General Plan
    2030 horizon year, resulting in the need to continue to thoughtfully manage growth
    and community character.
    The current and planned land use pattern in the City and surrounding planning
    area is schematically illustrated on Figure LU-1 (Community Form). This pattern is
    defined by the following form elements, all of which are the focus of General Plan
    policies:
    Established residential neighborhoods, several with a grid of tree-lined streets
    containing some of the first homes constructed in the City. The General Plan
    promotes preservation and enhancement of older neighborhoods.
    Newer suburban residential neighborhoods, many located within the City’s
    specific plan areas, consisting largely of single family homes with integrated parks
    and schools. The General Plan supports the creation of new neighborhoods that are
    diverse, walkable, and complete.
LU-2                 LAND USE AND COMMUNITY FORM

                                     City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
Figure LU-1: C O M M U N I T Y  FORM
                             LAND USE & COMMUNITY FORM                 LU-3
    City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
    Unique historic downtown business district that contributes to the City’s character
    and helps distinguish Oakdale from other surrounding valley towns. The General
    Plan advocates continued renewal of Downtown as a vital, pedestrian oriented
    place.
    Commercial corridors along the City’s major roadways, with an assorted pattern of
    local, regional, and tourist related services providing a hub of retail activity. The
    General Plan directs intensification, revitalization and expansion of commercial
    corridors.
    Large industrial and manufacturing presence concentrated in the southern portion
    of the City within and adjacent to the South Oakdale Industrial Specific Plan. The
    General Plan encourages a wide range of new and existing industries to create
    local jobs.
    Stanislaus River open space corridor winding along the northern edge of the City
    encompassing varied natural and visual resources. The General Plan provides for
    preservation of and improved public access to the Stanislaus River Corridor.
    Special planning areas within the City’s existing and proposed sphere of influence
    intended to be planned and annexed to address Oakdale’s additional growth
    opportunities through 2030. The General Plan directs well planned, regulated and
    sustainable new growth, and positions the City to proactively address the future
    North County Corridor (NCC).
    Greenbelt consisting of agricultural and rural lands separating Oakdale from
    Riverbank and the expanding Modesto area. The General Plan supports
    coordination with other agencies to ensure the retention of agricultural and rural
    lands around the City, including maintaining of the formal Oakdale and Riverbank
    Greenbelt west of the City.
    The General Plan promotes growth, revitalization and change beneficial to both the
    local economy and small town quality of life. It supports a balance of uses integral
    to sustaining revenue for the City to provide essential services, and directs a
    renewed inward focus on enhancing the City’s existing assets.
      Land Use Diagram
    The City’s Land Use Diagram (Figure LU-2) identifies the location of all land uses
    within the 2030 Planning Area. The Land Use Diagram is to be used and
    interpreted in conjunction with the text and policies included in the General Plan to
    direct land use decisions that integrate the community’s vision. Parcel level land use
    information may be obtained through the City’s Community Development and
    Services Department.

                                                       City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
   Land Use Designations
The City’s General Plan land use designations are broadly structured to allow for
flexibility in defined land use types, densities, and intensities to account for changes
in housing, employment, and retail characteristics over time. Typically a single land
use designation will be applied to a given area. However, to encourage a variety of
complementary uses and capture unique development opportunities, two or more
land use designations may be combined, and/or specialized land use designations
created, through an adopted specific plan.
Intensity standards are expressed as an allowable range of residential densities and                           RESIDENTIAL DENSITY

Community Form Principles and Design Expectations are identified in the Policy
Framework section of this element. The intent is to facilitate a common
understanding of the form, character, and quality of development desired in
Oakdale.
        INTERIM ZONING CONSISTENCY
        In areas where zoning has not been brought into conformity with the 2030 General
        Plan and a discretionary permit is required for development, the City shall allow
        property owners to either:
        1.   Develop consistent with existing zoning provided the City makes a finding that
             approval of the project would not interfere with the long-term development of the
             area consistent with the General Plan; or
        2.   Develop under the General Plan designation, in which case the City and property
             owners will facilitate a rezoning consistent with the General Plan.
        Any inconsistencies between the 2030 General Plan and City’s Zoning Code are to be
        remedied within a reasonable timeframe as required under Government Code Section
        65860 (c).

                                   City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
RESIDENTIAL NEIGHBORHOODS
                      Rural Estate (RE)
                      The RE designation accommodates large lot detached single-         Density: 2 or less dwelling
                      family homes intended to maintain a low density, rural living      units per gross acre
                      environment. This designation is to be applied in very limited
                      circumstances to address specific issues such as buffering
                      between agriculture and more dense residential neighborhoods,
                      and in other focused locations where large lot sizes are
                      appropriate and/or urban services are limited. The City may
                      approve the clustering of RE units to preserve significant open
                      space resources.
                      Very Low Density Residential (VLDR)
                      The VLDR designation accommodates detached single-family           Density: 2 to 5 dwelling
                      residential neighborhoods with generally larger to moderate lot    units per gross acre
                      sizes. This designation is to be applied sparingly as a transition
                      between rural and more intense uses, and in areas where
                      constraints or other considerations limit smaller lot development.
                      Low Density Residential (LDR)
                      The LDR designation accommodates traditional residential           Density: 4 to 8 dwelling
                      neighborhoods consisting predominantly of detached single-         units per gross acre
                      family homes on moderate to smaller lot sizes. Two family homes
                      and other attached housing types are also permitted. This
                      designation is applied to most neighborhoods in Oakdale and is
                      the City’s primary residential land use designation.
                      Medium Density Residential (MDR)
                      The MDR designation accommodates a variety of small lot single-    Density: 7 to 14 dwelling
                      family, two-family, and attached multiple-family residential       units per gross acre
                      housing types. This designation may be applied to promote a
                      diversity of housing choices within neighborhoods, and as a
                      transition to more intensive residential and non-residential uses.
                      High Density Residential (HDR)
                      The HDR designation accommodates higher density residential        Density: 14 or more
                      housing types including apartments, condominiums, and              dwelling units per gross
                      townhomes. This designation should be applied close to major       acre
                      transportation corridors, within and nearby the Downtown
                      District, and in proximity to commercial, employment, and
                      community service uses.
                     LAND USE & COMMUNITY FORM                                   LU-9

                     City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
COMMERCIAL, EMPLOYMENT & MIXED USE
                      Central Business District (CBD)
                      The CBD designation accommodates a unique mix of retail, service          Intensity: FAR 0.3 to 3.0
                      commercial, entertainment, restaurant, lodging, office, civic, and
                      potential upper floor residential uses. This designation is applied
                      within or near the Downtown District, and is intended to promote a
                      pedestrian-oriented and active street environment, as well as
                      enhance historic character.
                      General Commercial (GC)
                      The GC designation accommodates a broad range of local,                   Intensity: FAR 0.2 to 0.4
                      regional and visitor serving retail, office, civic and related uses. This
                      designation is applied to all commercial areas outside of the Central
                      Business District. Local serving GC areas should be sited to be easily
                      accessible by foot or bicycle to adjacent residents, with more
                      intensive GC areas located along major travel corridors.
                      Office (OFF)
                      The OFF designation accommodates professional, medical and                Intensity: FAR 0.3 to 0.7
                      general office uses. This designation may be applied to campus style
                      "office parks", and to residential-offices as transitional uses between
                      residential neighborhoods and higher intensity non-residential uses.
                      Accessory commercial and other office-serving uses may be
                      permitted.
                      Industrial (IND)
                      The IND designation accommodates a broad range of limited, light,         Intensity: FAR 0.2 to 0.5
                      and heavy industrial uses including manufacturing and assembly,
                      processing, warehousing and distribution, research and
                      development, office and other job creating uses. Supporting
                      commercial and other employee-serving uses are permitted.
                      Flex Use (FLEX)
                      The FLEX designation accommodates a potential mix of residential,         Intensity: As specified
                      commercial, employment or public uses on a single property. This          through an adopted
                      designation identifies a primary use, and possible secondary use(s).      Specific Plan or PD zone
                      Development may occur as either a vertical or horizontal mix of the
                      specified uses, or exclusively as one of the primary or secondary
                      uses. This designation is applied through an adopted specific plan
                      or Planned Development zone district.
                LU-10                     LAND USE AND COMMUNITY FORM
                                   City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
COMMUNITY FACILITIES, PARKS & RESOURCE USES
                       Public and Semi-Public (PSP)
                       The PSP designation is applied to public and semi-public facilities    Intensity: Varies
                       where the size or nature of such facilities warrants an individual
                       land use designation. This designation accommodates
                       government, public safety, utility, higher education, hospital,
                       cultural and other institutional buildings and facilities. Most public
                       and semi-public uses are also allowed within other land use
                       designations.
                       Airport (AP)
                       The AP designation is applied to the Oakdale Municipal Airport,        Intensity: Per City of
                       a general aviation facility owned and operated by the City,            Oakdale Municipal
                       located one mile southeast of Oakdale. In addition to airport          Airport Master Plan
                       facilities and aviation services, this designation may
                       accommodate uses that support or benefit from airport operations
                       such as commercial, office, industrial, and ancillary retail,
                       restaurants, and services.
                       School (SCH)
                       The SCH designation is applied to public, private, and religious       Intensity: NA
                       grade K through12 schools. The Oakdale Joint Unified School
                       District regulates the location, design, timing, and operation of
                       public schools within the City.
                       Park (P)
                       The P designation is applied to all lands where City park and          Intensity: NA
                       recreational facilities exist or are planned. Parks are to be
                       dedicated to the City and receive credit against the City’s
                       parkland dedication requirement. This designation may
                       accommodate active and passive park sites of various types and
                       configurations. In some specific plan areas, the park designation
                       is further refined to reflect unique uses or conditions (e.g.,
                       Park/Detention Basin).
                       Open Space (OS)
                       The OS designation is applied to permanently protect areas as          Intensity: NA
                       open space due to their habitat, scenic, historic, cultural or other
                       values. This designation is primarily allocated to lands along the
                       Stanislaus River corridor, and may incorporate passive recreation
                       and trails. In some specific plan areas, the open space
                       designation is further refined to reflect unique conditions (e.g.,
                       Landscape/Pedestrian Corridor). Open space may be owned by
                       the City, other public agency, or protected by easement. Lands
                       dedicated to the City may be granted partial credit against the
                       City’s parkland dedication requirement.
                      LAND USE & COMMUNITY FORM                                     LU-11

                      City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
SPECIAL PLANNING AREAS
                       Agriculture (AG)
                       The AG designation is applied to productive or potentially              Intensity: NA
                       productive agricultural lands, with typical minimum parcel sizes of
                       40 acres or larger. Uses include agricultural production, processing,
                       storage, distribution, on-site sales, farm and farmworker housing and
                       other compatible uses. This designation is primarily allocated to areas
                       north of the Stanislaus River and as an underlying land use to the
                       NCC Study Area.
                       Future Specific Plan Area
                       The Future Specific Plan Area designation is applied to those           Intensity: As specified
                       locations intended to accommodate new growth during the 2030            through future Specific
                       General Plan horizon. Each designated area is subject to future         Plans
                       comprehensive planning through a specific plan and annexation
                       into the City. City Council approval is required prior to initiating
                       planning of each Future Specific Plan Area. Agriculture is the
                       interim permitted use.
                       Area of Concern Overlay
                       The Area of Concern designation is applied to lands with underlying     Intensity: As specified
                       agricultural land use south and east of the City along potential        through future Specific
                       alignments being considered for the future NCC. The designation         Plans
                       does not denote any specific new land uses, but rather represents a
                       geographic area in which land use decisions or other governmental
                       actions of one local agency (the “Acting Agency” – Stanislaus
                       County) impact directly or indirectly upon another local agency (the
                       “Concerned Agency” – City of Oakdale). Upon reasonably
                       foreseeable implementation of the NCC, the City Council may
                       consider comprehensive planning of some or all of this area through
                       one or more specific plans, or may choose to retain lands for
                       agricultural or rural uses.
                 LU-12                    LAND USE AND COMMUNITY FORM

                                                City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
   Development Potential
General Plan land uses have been allocated considering projected future housing
and employment demands. The General Plan provides capacity beyond projected
2030 needs in order to maximize opportunities for new job and revenue generating
uses, and in recognition that not all lands will be available for development at a given
time. An adequate supply of employment and revenue generating land uses is a key
component of a healthy and prosperous community.
The estimated development potential allocated by the General Plan within the
Planning Area is illustrated on Figure LU-4 and summarized on Table LU-1. Given
typical variations in the intensity of individual projects, assumed “average” density
and FAR assumptions are applied for purposes of calculating development potential.
The resulting level of development is considered the “buildout” potential of the
General Plan.
The General Plan provides for a total of 15,504 dwelling units resulting in a
population of approximately 42,792 residents. The General Plan also provides for a
total of approximately 18 million square feet of nonresidential uses accommodating
approximately 31,849 employees. When jobs and housing are compared, the
General Plan provides for a ratio of 2.0 jobs for each housing unit (household).

                                               City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
  Policy Framework
LU-1: ORGANIZATION OF LAND USES
Goal LU-1
A balanced, well designed and sustainable land use pattern that
advances a high quality of life and promotes business opportunities.
 USE & FORM POLICIES
LU-1.1   Balance of Uses. Maintain a balance of land uses that allows residents the
         opportunity to live, work, shop and meet their daily needs in Oakdale. (RDR)
LU-1.2   Compact Form. Encourage a well-defined compact community form and
         adequate densities to allow for alternatives to vehicular travel. (RDR, NR-IP5)
LU-1.3   Compatibility. Require development to be compatible with surrounding
         uses, and complement Oakdale’s existing small town character and quality
         of life. (RDR)
LU-1.4   Employment and Retail Opportunities. Retain an ample supply of                       Also See Economic Vitality
         appropriately located land to promote a broad range of employment and                                 Element
         retail uses, strengthen the City's economic base, improve the balance between
         jobs and housing, and sustain a positive business climate. (RDR, LU-IP1)
LU-1.5   Infill and Underutilized Parcels. Give priority to the development of infill
         parcels and redevelopment of economically underutilized properties to
         accommodate a portion of projected future growth. (RDR, LU-IP2)
LU-1.6   Blueprint. Support new development and redevelopment that furthers the
         San Joaquin Valley Blueprint Smart Growth Principles. (RDR, IGC, LU-IP2,
         NR-IP5)
                                   LAND USE & COMMUNITY FORM                            LU-15

                                 City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
                                             COMMUNITY FORM PRINCIPLES
                                             San Joaquin Valley Smart Growth Principles
                                             The San Joaquin Valley Blueprint is the result of an unprecedented effort of the eight
                                             Valley Regional Planning Agencies to develop a long-term regional growth strategy for
                                             the future of the San Joaquin Valley. Adopted in April of 2009, the Blueprint presents a
                                             long range vision for a more efficient, sustainable, and livable future for the Valley. The
                                             Blueprint identifies the following 12 Smart Growth Principles to be used as a basis for
                                             future planning:
                                                   Create a range of housing opportunities and choices
                                                   Create walkable neighborhoods
                                                   Encourage community and stakeholder collaboration
                                                   Foster distinctive, attractive communities with a strong sense of place
                                                   Make development decisions predictable, fair, and cost-effective
                                                   Mix land uses
                                                   Preserve open space, farmland, natural beauty, and critical environmental areas
                                                   Provide a variety of transportation choices
                                                   Strengthen and direct development towards existing communities
                                                   Take advantage of compact building design
                                                   Enhance the economic vitality of the region
                                                   Support actions that encourage environmental resource management
                                  DESIGN POLICIES
                                 LU-1.7    Quality Design. Ensure a consistent level of high quality site, architectural
                                           and landscape design by adherence to design principles applied through
                                           the City’s design review process, specific plans, and Single-Family, Multiple-
                                           Family, and Industrial Design Expectations documents. (RDR)
                                             DESIGN EXPECTATIONS
                                             Citywide Design
     Oakdale’s existing Design               The City of Oakdale has adopted separate Single-Family, Multiple-Family, and
                                             Industrial Design Expectations. These Design Expectations are based upon the
Expectations documents can be
                                             Ahwahnee Principles and are intended to ensure that new development complements
  found on the City’s website at             and enhances the physical form of the community. The Design Expectations are to be
http://www.ci.oakdale.ca.us/.                applied with flexibility to encourage innovative, unique, and creative design.

LU-2: RESIDENTIAL NEIGHBORHOODS
Goal LU-2
Safe, attractive and walkable residential neighborhoods that provide
quality housing choices and are designed and maintained to enhance
livability.
  USE & FORM POLICIES
LU-2.1      Diverse Neighborhoods. Promote attractive and well maintained residential             Also See Housing Element
            neighborhoods through the review of development projects and
            prioritization of City capital and maintenance programs. Ensure that such
            neighborhoods provide a variety of housing types and densities targeting all
            income levels and stages of life. (RDR)
LU-2.2      Innovative Housing. Encourage development of innovative forms of housing
            that increase the diversity and affordability of housing options in the City.
            (RDR)
LU-2.3      Compatibility. Protect residential neighborhoods from the intrusion of
            potentially incompatible land uses and activities. Where non-residential uses
            are permitted adjacent to residential development, apply appropriate
            standards and treatments to ensure compatibility between uses without
            restricting foot and bicycle access. (RDR)
LU-2.4      Moderate and High Density Housing. Provide adequate moderate and high          Also See City of Oakdale Climate
            density housing to ensure that the City meets its Regional Housing Needs         Action Plan for additional GHG
            Allocation (RHNA). Locate such housing close to major transportation               reduction measures related to
            corridors, within the Downtown District, and in close proximity to                                      land use.
            commercial, employment, and community services. (RDR)                                 Also See Housing Element
LU-2.5      Historic Neighborhoods. Preserve and enhance the integrity, cohesiveness,
            and character of Oakdale’s historic neighborhoods including the street grid
            pattern, architectural styles, tree canopies, public improvements and
            amenities. (RDR, NR-IP7, NR-IP8)
LU-2.6      Neighborhood Revitalization. Promote revitalization of neighborhoods in
            need by maintaining public improvements, encouraging infill development
            compatible with the scale and character of existing development, and
            supporting public and private efforts to upgrade and maintain
            neighborhood appearance and the existing housing stock. (RDR, MP, LU-
            IP3)
LU-2.7      New Neighborhoods. Require new residential development and
            redevelopment to provide complete neighborhoods that compliment the
            ambience of the City’s older neighborhoods, and further the Residential
            Neighborhoods Community Form Principles. (RDR, NR-IP5)
                                     LAND USE & COMMUNITY FORM                           LU-17

                                 City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
                                            COMMUNITY FORM PRINCIPLES
                                            Residential Neighborhoods
                                            Neighborhoods in Oakdale are to provide a physical layout and land use mix that is
                                            well structured and complete, incorporating:
                                                  A mix of housing types and affordability
                                                  A variety of lot sizes, setbacks and alternating architectural styles, while still
                                                   accommodating production home development
                                                  A pedestrian friendly and walkable scale with wide sidewalks, shorter blocks,
                                                   narrower street sections and well marked crosswalks
                                                  Integrated parks, open space, schools, local retail and other gathering and
                                                   activity areas within convenient walking and bicycling distance of residents and
                                                   located as neighborhood focal points where feasible
                                                  Interconnected street, transit, pedestrian and bicycle facilities linking multiple
                                                   destinations within and between neighborhoods
                                                  Extensive tree canopies and attractive landscaping that enhance neighborhood
                                                   character and streetscapes
                                                  Front doors, stoops, and porches oriented to the primary street frontage
                                                  Building designs and orientations that minimize the visual prominence of
                                                   garages and parking areas, including use of alleys as appropriate
                                                  Units fronting versus backing on to parks and open space as practical
                                                  Responsiveness to topography, biological, and other constraints
                                                  Designs and measures that promote energy efficiency, low impact development
                                                   and help to reduce development’s carbon footprint consistent with the City’s
                                                   Climate Action Plan
                                  DESIGN POLICIES
                                 LU-2.8    Quality Residential Design. Ensure a consistent level of high quality
                                           residential design by adherence to the design principles applied through the
                                           City’s design review process, specific plans, and Residential Design
                                           Expectations. (RDR)
                                            DESIGN EXPECTATIONS
                                            Residential Neighborhoods
     Oakdale’s existing Design              The City of Oakdale has adopted Single-Family and Multiple-Family Design
                                            Expectations intended to ensure quality residential neighborhoods that are enduring

                                            and sustainable. The Design Expectations establish Specific Plan Design Principles

Goal LU-3
An enhanced people oriented Downtown District that serves as the
centerpiece of community identity, activity, and culture.
 USE, FORM & DESIGN POLICIES
LU-3.1   City Core. Preserve and strengthen the vitality of Downtown Oakdale as the
         City’s civic, social and symbolic core. (RDR, LU-IP4)
LU-3.2   Mixed Use. Encourage development and redevelopment of Downtown as a                Also See City of Oakdale Climate
         unique, pedestrian oriented shopping district with a compatible mix of local         Action Plan for additional GHG
         and regional serving retail, office, residential, restaurant, civic, and cultural      reduction measures related to
         uses. (RDR, LU-IP2, LU-IP3, LU-IP4)                                                                         land use.
LU-3.3   Housing Opportunities. Support the development of new housing within
         Downtown including mixed use projects that integrate upper floor housing
         with commercial, office and other uses. (RDR, LU-IP2, LU-IP3, LU-IP4)
LU-3.4   Civic Functions. Maintain a Downtown presence for government and other
         civic functions. (RDR, LU-IP4)
LU-3.5   Cultural Activities. Support the development of community oriented cultural
         facilities and events that attract residents and visitors to Downtown. (RDR, JP,
         LU-IP4)
LU-3.6   Consolidated Parking. Promote the development of on-street and
         centralized parking facilities that can be shared by multiple users, including
         exploring the feasibility of a parking district or other methods of
         consolidated parking. (RDR, LU-IP4)
LU-3.7   Historic Buildings. Preserve and enhance buildings of historic and
         architectural importance, and ensure that new development is compatible
         with and contributes to the historic identity of Downtown. (RDR, LU-IP4, NR-
         IP7, NR-IP8)
LU-3.8   Specific Plan. Prepare a Downtown Specific Plan to direct the future use and
         revitalization of the Downtown District. (see Figure LU-5) (LU-IP4, NR-IP5)

                                        Downtown Specific Plan
                                        The Downtown Specific Plan is to be developed with the active participation of local

                                        Specific Plan is to incorporate design expectations that enhance the areas historic and

                                             Economic development and vitality
                                             Pedestrian circulation, vehicular traffic and transit
                                             Parking strategies
                                             Infrastructure and public service
                                             Historic resource preservation
                                             Design expectations
                                             Implementation, funding and maintenance programs
                                             Incentives, branding and promotions strategies
                                             Targeted Planning Area opportunities defined later in this element

                                         funding.

 USE & FORM POLICIES

                COMMUNITY FORM PRINCIPLES
                Commercial & Mixed Use Development
                Commercial, office and mixed use development in Oakdale is to provide a physical
                layout that is well designed, incorporating:
                     Landscaping, building orientations, architectural detail, massing, lighting and
                      other treatments that enhance street frontages and compliment adjacent
                      residential uses
                     Buildings oriented to the street with minimal setbacks, front entries, transparent
                      storefronts, and rear parking where a pedestrian environment exists or is desired
                     Pedestrian and bicycle access to surrounding neighborhoods
                     Connectivity to transit facilities where present
                     Designs, orientations, landscaping and other measures to reduce the visual
                      prominence of parking areas and provide shading
                     Building elevations and rooflines articulated on all sides visible from streets and
                      other public spaces
                     Development measures that will ensure drive-through and “big box” commercial
                      uses are compatible with the character of adjoining uses
                     Designs and measures that promote energy efficiency, low impact development
                      and help to reduce development’s carbon footprint consistent with the City’s
                      Climate Action Plan
                     Targeted Planning Area opportunities defined later in this element

               consistent high quality design. (LU-IP5)

                address:

                     Landscaping, shading and hardscapes
                     Lighting and signage
                     Building materials, architecture, facade articulation, entries and roofs
                     Walls, fencing and screening
                     Service areas and equipment
                     Targeted Planning Area opportunities defined later in this element
                The preparation of the commercial, office and mixed use Design Expectations is
                contingent upon the availability of funding.

     LU-7: SPHERE OF INFLUENCE & SPECIFIC PLANS
     Goal LU-7
     New growth areas that are carefully planned, efficient, and contribute
     positively to the community.
      SOI POLICIES
     LU-7.1    Vacant and Underutilized Lands. Promote development of vacant and
               underutilized lands within the City prior to entertaining annexation of new
               growth areas. (RDR, LU-IP2)
     LU-7.2    Annexation Proposals. Support annexation proposals that: 1) are adjacent to
               and provide for logical expansion of the City; 2) are designated by the
               General Plan for potential future growth or specific conservation purposes; 3)
               provide for the efficient extension of City infrastructure and services; 4)
               contribute positively to the City’s fiscal viability and quality of life. (RDR)
     LU-7.3    Revenue Sharing. Update/renew the Master Property Tax Agreement with
               Stanislaus County to secure an appropriate allocation of property tax to the
               City when annexations occur. (OFB, IGC)
     LU-7.4    Pre-annexation Development Proposals. Coordinate with Stanislaus County
               to ensure that land use and development proposals considered by the County
               within the City’s SOI prior to annexation by Oakdale do not conflict with the
               City’s long term plans and development standards. (RDR, IGC)
      SPECIFIC PLAN POLICIES
     LU-7.5    Specific Plans. Require preparation and approval of a specific plan for each
               future growth and annexation area to direct comprehensive and orderly
               planning. (RDR, NR-IP5)
     LU-7.6    Initiation of Specific Plans. Secure City Council approval prior to initiating a
               specific plan. Such approval shall consider: 1) market demand for the
               proposed land uses; 2) potential benefits and impacts to the City; 3)
               capacity to provide and fund a full range of public infrastructure and
               services; and 4) ability to comply with General Plan policies. (RDR)
     LU-7.7    Concurrent Approvals. Require preparation and approval of the following
               as applicable, concurrent or in series with a specific plan:
                   General Plan Amendment and               Public Facilities Financing Plan

                   Zoning Entitlements and                  Design Guidelines

                   Environmental Impact Report              LAFCO entitlements (RDR, IGC)
                   Infrastructure Master Plans
                   Fiscal Impact Analysis

LU-8: AGRICULTURAL GREENBELT
Goal LU-8
Productive agricultural and open space lands surrounding Oakdale
and providing a distinct separation from adjacent communities.
 GREENBELT POLICIES
LU-8.1   Greenbelt. Maintain agricultural and rural lands around the perimeter of
         Oakdale’s Planning Area as a physical separator from nearby urbanizing
         areas. (RDR, IGC)
LU-8.2   Oakdale-Riverbank Corridor Area Agreement. Continue to enforce and
         extend the term of the Oakdale and Riverbank Corridor Area Agreement.
         (RDR, IGC)
           COMMUNITY FORM PRINCIPLES
           Oakdale & Riverbank Corridor Area Agreement
           In June 2001 the Cities of Oakdale and Riverbank, along with Stanislaus County, entered
           into an agreement to retain a greenbelt along the Highway 108 corridor between the two
           communities. This agreement reflects the interests of all the parties to maintain a
           separation between Oakdale and Riverbank, and to preserve the rural character of the
           Highway 108 corridor. The agreement specifies that within the greenbelt area:
                 The County will maintain agricultural land uses
                 The Cities will not request extensions of their respective SOIs
                 The parties will encourage agricultural landowners to enter non-regulatory
                  programs, such as the Williamson act, to conserve farmland
                 The parties will consider planning and developing non-motorized trails between
                  the communities
                 The agreement was extended in June 2011 for a 10 year period through June
                  2021.
