                            City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
Also See Natural Resources LU-8.3     Retain Agricultural Uses. Work with Stanislaus County and other applicable
                   Element            agencies to retain viable agricultural and rural uses within and adjacent to
                                      Oakdale’s Planning Area, and to discourage small lot land divisions and
                                      other activities that could interfere with implementation of the City’s General
                                      Plan. (RDR, IGC)
                            LU-8.4    Urban-Rural Edge. Require new development along the planned urban
                                      edge of the City to provide a clearly distinct boundary and protect the
                                      ongoing viability of adjacent agriculture. (RDR)
                                        COMMUNITY FORM PRINCIPLES
                                        Urban-Rural Edge
                                        New development along the planned urban edge of the City adjacent to active
                                        agricultural land designated for long-term production may be required to provide
                                        buffer treatments as appropriate. The intent is to ensure treatments adequate to
                                        minimize the impacts of new development on agricultural operations, and the impacts
                                        of agriculture on new development. Buffer treatments may consist of transitional lot
                                        sizes, setbacks, walls, berming, vegetative screens, and other measures provided by
                                        the new development. The width, type and ongoing maintenance of buffer treatments
                                        will be as specified in an adopted Specific Plan or as otherwise determined on a
                                        case‐by‐case basis, considering crop types, agricultural practices, prevailing winds,
                                        topography, the type of development and other relevant factors. Buffer setback areas
                                        may include roadways, utilities, drainage facilities, trails, parks, landscaping, open
                                        space and other uses compatible with ongoing agricultural operations. Where feasible,
                                        views to adjacent agricultural lands from new development should be retained.
                             GOVERNMENTAL COORDINATION POLICIES
                            LU-8.5    Planning Coordination. Ensure that City planning activities are coordinated
                                      with other affected or responsible government agencies as appropriate.
                                      (RDR, IGC)
                            LU-8.6    Regional Planning. Participate in regional planning efforts with the
                                      Stanislaus Council of Governments, San Joaquin Valley Air Pollution Control
                                      District and other appropriate organizations to ensure that City issues and
                                      interests are represented. (IGC)
                            LU-8.7    Project Review. Review, comment, and coordinate on plans and projects of
                                      overlapping or neighboring agencies to ensure compatibility with the City’s
                                      General Plan, and to make certain that impacts on the City are mitigated.
                                      (RDR, IGC)

                                              City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
   Targeted Planning Areas
The Targeted Planning Areas provide additional direction for specific locations in
Oakdale that will accommodate much of the City’s anticipated redevelopment and
potential new growth. These areas are organized into two broad categories:
1. Opportunity Corridors
2. Special Planning Areas
Direction in this section expresses specific intentions for use, design and character
unique to each area. This direction supplements and is in addition to other General
Plan policies.
LU-9: OPPORTUNITY CORRIDORS
The opportunity corridors are located along Oakdale’s major roadways, F Street
and Yosemite Avenue. These corridors are a key part of the City’s urban framework
and contain a varied mix of residential, commercial, industrial and related uses
including some sites that are vacant, underutilized, or in need of revitalization. The
opportunity exists to remake the corridors into more intense, efficient, pedestrian
friendly and visually pleasing destinations. The intent is to bring new vitality to the
areas, encouraging public and private investment/reinvestment. Some of the areas
beyond these corridors are specifically addressed in several of the City’s specific
plans.
For planning purposes the corridors have been divided into seven distinct segments
(see Figure LU-6). Each segment has a slightly different set of conditions, challenges,
and opportunities. Although the linear extent of each corridor segment is defined, its
depth from the adjacent roadway purposely is not. In general, the direction in this
section applies to properties that directly front the corridors and any adjacent
properties that could reasonably contribute to achieving defined objectives. This
depth will vary dependent upon existing and planned uses, lotting patterns,
adjacent street connections, and the specific objectives for the corridor.
While the General Plan provides broad direction and intent for each corridor, it is
anticipated that more detailed plans will be prepared for each corridor either
individually or in combination to further define objectives, strategies and specific
actions. All subsequent planning efforts should actively engage local business
owners and residents to address land use; market feasibility/strategies; mobility;
utilities/infrastructure; streetscapes; parking; design guidelines and development
standards; regulatory strategies and incentives; and implementation/financing
programs. The City will pursue grants and other available funding sources to help
finance such planning efforts.

                              City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan

WEST F STREET CORRIDOR
Location:
F Street from the Bridle Ridge and Crane Crossing Specific Plans on the west, to
Mann and Blankenship Avenues on the east. This section of F Street (SR 108) is
scheduled for relinquishment from state to local control. The south side of F Street
from Wood Avenue east was within the City’s Redevelopment Area (as it existed).
Adopted Land Use:
General Commercial (GC) with a mix of Low Density Residential (LDR) and Medium
Density Residential (MDR)
Existing Character:
F Street consists of 2 lanes with an unimproved median, widening to 4 lanes west of
Lee Avenue. There is significant variation in paved street width and pedestrian
improvements along the corridor. Residential uses and some vacant properties exist
to the west, transitioning to a scattered mix of retail, service, and related uses
traveling east. Parcel sizes are varied, with many lots on the north side of the
corridor oriented at an angle to F Street. The style and quality of development varies
representing different eras of growth with no consistent architectural, setback,
landscaping, signage or streetscape treatments. Both the Riverbank Oakdale Transit
Authority (ROTA) and Stanislaus Regional Transit (StaRT) have transit routes along
this corridor.
Planning Opportunities:
Development and redevelopment along the West F Street Corridor should promote
the following:
New uses and businesses, including opportunities for:
    Local serving retail, offices and services.
                                    LAND USE & COMMUNITY FORM                         LU-31

     City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
         Additional higher density residential and mixed use development.
         Possible senior housing and medical related services in proximity to the
          hospital.
     Beautification and intensification of development, including opportunities for:
         Renovation and reuse of existing development through façade
          improvements, upgraded landscaping, consistent signage, screening and
          shading of parking areas, and improved pedestrian access from streets to
          storefronts.
         Consolidation of small, underutilized lots into larger parcels to support viable
          and cohesive development projects.
         Creation of distinct mixed use activity nodes in key locations along the corridor
          distinguished by their mix of uses, intensity, compact development form, and
          greater emphasis on pedestrian and transit access.
         New development that presents a storefront along the corridor with building
          frontages oriented to and framing the street, minimal setbacks, front entries,
          transparent storefronts, appropriate building heights, and interior parking lot
          configurations.
      Pedestrian mobility and alternative transportation modes, including opportunities
      to:
         Improve and widen sidewalks and eliminate unimproved gaps west of Oak
          Avenue.
         Enhance pedestrian crosswalks to safely accommodate pedestrians and cyclists
          through clearly identified markings and/or pavers, bulb-outs at corners (where
          on-street parking exists), signage and traffic controls, in particular near the high
          school and other locations expected to generate significant pedestrian traffic.
         Add bike routes along the full length of the corridor.
         Consolidate driveways to minimize curb cuts.
         Enhance transit stops, shelters and connectivity to corridor uses.
         Strengthen pedestrian linkages to adjacent neighborhoods.
      Attractive, functional and maintainable streetscapes, including opportunities to:
         Add street trees to maximize shade and sense of enclosure.
         Provide enhanced and consistent landscaping, street furnishings, paving
          materials, public signage, and lighting.
         Underground overhead utility lines west of Oak Street.
         Incorporate signage, decorative banners and other techniques to create a
          unique identity for the corridor.
         Ensure consistent property, landscape and public improvement maintenance.

F STREET HISTORIC TRANSITION CORRIDOR
Location:
F Street from Mann and Blankenship Avenues on the west, to 1st Avenue on the
east. This section of F Street (SR 108) is scheduled for relinquishment from state to
local control. The majority of this corridor was within the City’s Redevelopment Area
(as it existed), with the exception of the north side of F Street west of Laurel Avenue.
Adopted Land Use:
Central Business District (CBD) with General Commercial (GC) on the west end.
Existing Character:
F Street consists of 2 lanes with an unimproved median. Sidewalks exist on both
sides of the road, with a varied and inconsistent street tree pattern. Uses include
older, historic homes on the west and east ends of the corridor that have been
converted to business uses, with a mix of more traditional retail and services uses
sandwiched in between. Prominent church buildings also exist, with a newer retail
center at the northeast corner of Laurel Avenue. The style and quality of
development varies along the corridor between the converted residential structures
and the more traditional retail development. Both ROTA and StaRT have transit
routes along this corridor.
Planning Opportunities:
Development and redevelopment along the F Street Historic Transition Corridor
should promote the following:
                                     LAND USE & COMMUNITY FORM                          LU-33

     City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
     New uses and businesses, including opportunities for:
         An eclectic mix of local serving retail, services and professional offices.
         Additional higher density residential and mixed use development, particularly
          in proximity to downtown.
     Beautification and intensification of development, including opportunities for:
         Continued conversion of homes to business uses along and adjacent to the
          corridor, with retention of residential character through preservation and
          enhancement of building architecture and scale, large front yard setbacks,
          extensive landscaping, and use of on-street or rear access parking.
         Renovation and reuse of existing retail structures through façade improvements,
          upgraded landscaping, and consistent signage that complements the scale and
          character of the adjacent residential businesses.
         New development that replicates and is compatible with the existing
          development pattern in form, scale, orientation, setbacks, parking, architectural
          style and landscape treatments.
      Pedestrian mobility and alternative transportation modes, including opportunities
      to:
         Improve and widen sidewalks.
         Enhance pedestrian crosswalks to safely accommodate pedestrians and cyclists
          through clearly identified markings and/or pavers, bulb-outs at corners (where
          on-street parking exists), signage and traffic controls, in particular at locations
          expected to generate significant pedestrian traffic.
         Add bike routes along the full length of the corridor.
         Enhance transit stops, shelters and connectivity to corridor uses.
         Strengthen pedestrian linkages to adjacent neighborhoods.
      Attractive, functional and maintainable streetscapes, including opportunities to:
         Maximize on-street parking opportunities.
         Incorporate distinctive and consistent streetscape treatments that tie the corridor
          together, acting as a bridge between the converted residential uses on the west
          and east ends, and include street trees, enhanced landscaping, street
          furnishings, paving materials, and public signage.
         Extend the existing historic acorn street lighting fixtures found east of South
          Church Street west to Mann and Blankenship Avenues.
         Incorporate signage, decorative banners and other techniques to create a
          unique identity for the corridor in itself and as an entry to Downtown.
         Extend street tree and other streetscape design elements one block (or more)
          down cross streets to create a more cohesive district.

DOWNTOWN CORRIDOR
Location:
F Street from 1st Avenue on the west to 4th Avenue on the east. Yosemite Avenue
from D Street on the north to G Street on the south. The entirety of this corridor was
within the City’s Redevelopment Area (as it existed). F Street west of Yosemite
Avenue (SR 108) is scheduled for relinquishment from state to local control.
Adopted Land Use:
Central Business District (CBD) with Public and Semi-Public (PSP).
                                  LAND USE & COMMUNITY FORM                           LU-35

     City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
     Existing Character:
     The F Street and Yosemite Avenue crossing is the central intersection in Oakdale,
     carrying significant volumes of local and regional traffic. Both roadways are 4
     lanes, with the exception of F Street west of Yosemite Avenue which is 2 lanes.
     There is a railroad crossing of F Street just to the east of its intersection with
     Yosemite Avenue. Development consists primarily of older historic structures with
     traditional storefronts at back of wide sidewalks, street trees, and a variety of
     intimate scaled retail, restaurants, office and civic uses. This pattern begins to
     dissipate east of Yosemite Avenue. Included within the Downtown Corridor is the
     Gene Bianchi Community Center and C.E. Wood Park. ROTA and StaRT have
     transit routes along F Street and on Yosemite Avenue south of F Street. The
     Downtown Corridor will be included in the future Downtown District Specific Plan.
     Planning Opportunities:
     Development and redevelopment along the Downtown Corridor should promote the
     following:
     New uses and businesses, including opportunities for:
         A broader mix of local and regional serving retail, goods, services, offices,
          restaurants and entertainment.
         Additional higher density housing and mixed use development with residential
          units over commercial uses.
         Community oriented cultural facilities and events that attract residents and
          visitors to downtown.
         City administrative buildings to maintain a local government presence.
      Beautification and intensification of development, including opportunities for:
         Renovation and reuse of existing historic structures in a manner that preserves
          and enhances the historic and architectural character of the buildings.
         Renovation and reuse of existing retail structures on the east side of Yosemite
          Avenue north of F Street, and on F Street east of Yosemite Avenue, through
          façade improvements, upgraded landscaping, and consistent signage that
          replicates and complements the scale and character of Downtown.
         New development that is compatible with and contributes to the historic identity
          and pedestrian nature of Downtown in form, scale, orientation, setbacks,
          transparency of storefronts, parking, architectural style and landscape treatments.
      Pedestrian mobility and alternative transportation modes, including opportunities to:
         Improve and widen sidewalks in particular on the east side of Yosemite Avenue
          north of F Street, and on F Street east of Yosemite Avenue.
         Enhance pedestrian crosswalks to safely accommodate pedestrians and cyclists
          through clearly identified markings and/or pavers, bulb-outs at corners,
          signage and traffic controls.
         Enhance transit stops, shelters and connectivity to corridor uses.
         Strengthen pedestrian linkages to adjacent neighborhoods.

     EAST F STREET CORRIDOR
     Location:
     F Street from 4th Avenue on the west to the East F Street Specific Plan on the east. The
     majority of this corridor wass within the City’s Redevelopment Area (as it existed).
     Adopted Land Use:
     General Commercial with Central Business District on the west end.
     Existing Character:
     F Street consists of 4 lanes with an unimproved median. This section of F Street (SR
     108/120) accommodates significant volumes of regional traffic, some passing
     through the City to and from the Sierras. The development pattern consists of a
     variety of auto-oriented strip commercial uses and centers, most set back from the
     street with front parking lots and no consistent architectural, landscaping, and
     streetscape treatments, or clear relationships between developments. Some of these
     uses and centers are older, some newer, and some underutilized and in need of
     upgrades. There are numerous freestanding signs of various sizes and character
     that are visually prominent along portions of the corridor. Uses include local,
     regional, and visitor serving retail, services, lodging, gas stations, auto sales and
     similar uses. There is a StaRT transit route along this corridor.

NORTH YOSEMITE GATEWAY CORRIDOR
Location:
Yosemite Avenue from the Stanislaus River on the north to E Street on the south. The
majority of this corridor was within the City’s Redevelopment Area (as it existed).
Adopted Land Use:
A mix of General Commercial (GC), Medium Density Residential (MDR), Park (P),
and Industrial (IND).
Existing Character:
Yosemite Avenue is a key entry and travel route in the City, transitioning from 3
lanes just south of the Stanislaus River Bridge, to 4 lanes along the remainder of the
corridor. Yosemite Avenue (SR 120) connects Bay Area and Central Valley cities
                                    LAND USE & COMMUNITY FORM                         LU-41

     City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
     with several Sierra Nevada foothill towns and Yosemite National Park. As a result,
     this roadway experiences large volumes of tourism-related traffic during the summer
     and shoulder seasons. The development pattern is extremely mixed and fractured.
     The east side of Yosemite Avenue includes a landscaped corridor (Cottle Multi-Use
     Trail) south of the bridge followed by industrial uses that immediately abut the
     roadway with no sidewalks or landscaping. The west side includes a suburban
     shopping center set far back from the street, older commercial buildings with lesser
     setbacks and limited landscaping, apartments, auto repair, and a vacant parcel.
     The style and quality of development varies substantially with no consistent
     architectural, setback, landscaping, signage or streetscape treatments.
     Planning Opportunities:
     Development and redevelopment along the North Yosemite Gateway Corridor
     should promote the following:
     New uses and businesses, including opportunities for:
         Local serving and regional retail, offices and services.
         Travel and Tourism uses including related retail, restaurant, services and lodging.
         Additional higher density residential and mixed use development, particularly
          in proximity to downtown.
      Beautification and intensification of development, including opportunities for:
         Renovation and reuse of existing development through façade improvements,
          upgraded landscaping, consistent signage, screening and shading of parking
          areas, and improved pedestrian access from streets to storefronts.
         Integration of the industrial uses on the east side of the roadway into the
          corridor. Designs should embrace rather than attempt to hide these uses,
          incorporating aesthetic and functional upgrades to enhance appearance,
          improve vehicular access, and allow pedestrians and bicyclists to conveniently
          and safely enter and pass by the sites.
         New development that presents a storefront along the corridor with building
          frontages oriented to and framing the street, minimal setbacks, front entries,
          transparent storefronts, appropriate building heights, and interior parking lot
          configurations, in particular in proximity to Downtown.
         Enhanced views of and connectivity to the Stanislaus River corridor for uses
          adjacent to that corridor.
      Pedestrian mobility and alternative transportation modes, including opportunities to:
         Improve and widen sidewalks, in particular on the east side of the roadway
          connecting to the Class 1 bike trail (Cottle Multi-Use Trail) within the landscaped
          corridor.
         Provide trail access to the Stanislaus River and participate in the implementation
          of the Stanislaus River Corridor Access Plan (also see Community Services
          Element).
         Enhance pedestrian crosswalks to safely accommodate pedestrians and cyclists
          through clearly identified markings and/or pavers, signage and traffic controls,
          in particular at locations expected to generate significant pedestrian traffic.
         Add bike routes along the full length of the corridor.
         Strengthen pedestrian linkages to adjacent neighborhoods.

     SOUTH YOSEMITE INDUSTRIAL CORRIDOR
     Location:
     Yosemite Avenue from J Street on the north to the South Oakdale Industrial Specific
     Plan to the south. The majority of this corridor was within the City’s Redevelopment
     Area (as it existed), with the exception of the very southern section near Warnerville

LU-46                  LAND USE AND COMMUNITY FORM

                                               City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
Adopted Land Use:
Industrial (IND) with General Commercial (GC), Public Semi-Public and
FLEX/Industrial (FLEX/IND) north of Greger Street.
Existing Character:
Yosemite Avenue consists of a combination of 2 lane and 4 lane segments, with
bike routes along a portion of the corridor. This roadway provides a major linkage
between Oakdale and the nearby cities on Modesto, Ceres and Turlock, as well as
potential connection to the future NCC. There are crossings of the Burlington
Northern Santa Fe and Union Pacific rail lines south of J Street. The development
pattern includes a combination of large industrial uses, specifically ConAgra and
Sconza (chocolate manufacturer), as well as business parks, gas stations, scattered
residential uses, and large vacant parcels. Sidewalks are limited, with varied
building setbacks and landscaping. In some cases buildings, parking lots and
fencing are located immediately adjacent to the roadway. There is a StaRT transit
route along this corridor.
Planning Opportunities:
Development and redevelopment along the South Yosemite Industrial Corridor
should promote the following:
New uses and businesses, including opportunities for:
   A diverse range of industrial and business park users.
   Employee serving commercial uses and businesses, consolidated into easily
    accessible activity nodes along Yosemite Avenue when feasible.
Beautification and intensification of development, including opportunities for:
   Renovation and reuse of existing development through façade improvements,
    upgraded landscaping, consistent signage, screening and shading of parking
    areas, and improved pedestrian access from streets to building entries.
   To the extent appropriate, new development may be subject to development
    standards and design guidelines compatible with those from the South Oakdale
    Industrial Specific Plan (as may be modified by the City). Such requirements
    address lot configurations, setbacks, landscape corridors and plantings, street
    trees and lighting, pedestrian facilities, building massing, screening, walls and
    fences, signage, energy efficiency and other provisions to enhance street
    frontages and minimize impacts to adjacent uses.
   Development of distinct employee serving commercial activity nodes in key
    locations along Yosemite Avenue distinguished by their mix of uses and
    emphasis on pedestrian access.
Pedestrian mobility and alternative transportation modes, including opportunities to:
   Improve and widen sidewalks and eliminate unimproved gaps.
   Enhance pedestrian crosswalks to safely accommodate pedestrians and cyclists
    through clearly identified markings and/or pavers, signage and traffic controls,
    in particular at locations expected to generate significant pedestrian traffic such
    as commercial nodes.
   Add bike routes along the full length of the corridor consistent with the cross
    sections specified in the South Oakdale Industrial Specific Plan.

     City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
        Enhance transit stops, shelters and connectivity to corridor uses.
        Strengthen pedestrian linkages to adjacent neighborhoods.

LU-10: SPECIAL PLANNING AREAS
The Special Planning Areas are within the City’s existing and proposed sphere of
influence and are intended to be planned/annexed to address Oakdale’s potential
growth and conservation opportunities through 2030. Included are Future Specific
Plan Areas 2, 5, and 8, as well as the Area of Concern (See Figure LU-7). The City
Council may initiate a specific plan for one or more of the Special Planning Areas
based upon market demand, potential benefits and impacts to the City, capacity to
provide and fund a full range of public infrastructure and services, and ability to
comply with all other General Plan policies.

     south and east of the City. Once a final alignment is selected, the City may adjust
     the boundary of the Area of Concern to encompass those lands most appropriately
     planned by Oakdale. Upon the reasonably foreseeable implementation of the NCC,
     the City may initiate one or more specific plans to comprehensively plan all or a
     portion of the Area of Concern, or may choose to retain all or a portion of the lands
     for agricultural or rural uses.

     principles and all other requirements of the General Plan. In initiating a specific
     plan within the NCC Study Area, the City Council shall provide planning direction
     that includes:
         The appropriate boundary of the specific plan and annexation area. The
          boundary shall consider natural and man-made barriers, and be of sufficient
          size to allow for comprehensive planning of the defined area.

              City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
              LU-11: FRINGE COMMUNITIES
              Several unincorporated neighborhoods located within Oakdale’s SOI are
              considered “fringe” communities, as shown on Figure LU-8. Unincorporated fringe
              communities are inhabited and unincorporated territory that is within a city’s SOI as
              defined by State law (SB 244). It is anticipated that requests for annexation of these
              communities into the City of Oakdale may be considered during the planning
              horizon of the 2030 General Plan. Annexation requests are subject to approval of
              the Stanislaus County Local Agency Formation Commission (LAFCO), and involve
              an evaluation and determination of the City’s ability to serve the lands proposed for
              annexation, including the fringe communities. The following provides a general
              analysis of water, wastewater, stormwater drainage, and structural fire protection
              needs or deficiencies for each of the unincorporated fringe communities, along with
              an analysis of potential funding mechanisms that could make the extension of
              facilities and services to identified communities financially feasible. This analysis is
              intended as a starting point for potential future LAFCO study.
Figure LU-8: U N I N C O R P O R A T E D F R I N G E C O M M U N I T I E S
         LU-58                  LAND USE AND COMMUNITY FORM

                                                City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
Sunset Park Subdivision
This 30 parcel community is comprised of single-family residences located on Iris
Avenue, Charles Street and Magnolia Street adjacent to Oak Avenue on the west
side of Oakdale. At approximately 10.5 acres, the Sunset Park Subdivision is
situated just west of the City limits, but within the existing SOI and Crane Crossing
Specific Plan (CCSP). Land in this community is currently used and designated for
Very Low Density Residential uses at 2 to 5 dwelling units per acre under the
Specific Plan and 2030 General Plan. Also included within the community are a
church/private school, and several vacant lots.
Water
Domestic water is provided by the City of Oakdale from a point of connection in
Oak Avenue. The extent and size of the distribution mainlines is unknown.
Upgrading the system to meet City standards will require installation of
approximately seven fire hydrants throughout the community.
Wastewater
All of the parcels within the Sunset Park Subdivision use private septic systems, with
no public sanitary sewer services provided.
In order for the City of Oakdale to provide sewer service to this community it will be
necessary to construct approximately 1,770 feet of 8-inch sanitary sewer mainline,
30 services, four manholes and cleanouts as needed. The point of connection for
sanitary sewer service will be an existing stub at Oak Avenue.
Storm Drainage
All storm drainage is collected by roll over curb and gutter. Runoff flows to several
drainage inlets within the community. Each drainage inlet appears to be connected
directly to its own drywell for subsurface percolation. Additional storm drainage
improvements are not anticipated.
Dry Utilities
The site is served by underground electrical, cable, natural gas, and telephone.
Fire Protection
The Oakdale Rural Fire Protection District currently provides emergency and fire
protection services to the greater Oakdale area (outside of the City limits). Upon
annexation this community would be served by the City of Oakdale Fire
Department. The nearest fire station to this community is Station #4 located at 450
South Willowood Drive in the City of Oakdale.
Access
Access to this community is available via Oak Avenue and is sufficient for public
safety services.
                                     LAND USE & COMMUNITY FORM                        LU-59
     City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
     Financing
     As the Sunset Park Subdivision is part of the CCSP, annexation of this existing
     community would occur as part of a larger application to be submitted and
     approved by the Stanislaus County LAFCO. A Fiscal Analysis/Public Facilities
     Financing Plan was prepared for the entire Specific Plan area. The Fiscal Analysis
     assesses impacts of development on the City’s ability to fund services. Overall,
     development of CCSP would be adequately provided public services and utilities by
     the City of Oakdale after annexation. The Public Facilities Financing Plan identifies
     the cost of backbone infrastructure to serve the specific plan area, the allocation and
     ability of properties to support those costs, and the potential funding and financing
     mechanisms that can used to construct the infrastructure and facilities for the CCSP.
     Based on this Financing Plan, a Financing Strategy was prepared.
     Potential financing mechanisms to improve water and wastewater infrastructure to
     City standards in this community include: city impact fees; developer financing;
     and/or a community facilities district (CFD). The City of Oakdale has adopted a set
     of development impact fees (i.e., Community Facilities Fees) to finance capital
     improvements. Fees are collected prior to issuance of a building permit for water,
     wastewater, storm drainage, and fire protection. Future updates to the City’s fee
     program may include certain improvements within the CCSP and Sunset Park
     Subdivision. Developer financing is another mechanism that may be required to
     fund backbone infrastructure and facilities for shortfall financing and for in-tract
     subdivision improvements. A CFD may also be established to help pay for
     improvements. The 1982 Mello-Roos Community Facilities Act enables cities to
     establish a CFD to fund various facilities and services. The proceeds from a CFD
     bond sale can be used for direct funding of improvements. Other fee, assessment or
     alternative funding mechanisms focused upon improvements associated with the
     Sunset Park Subdivision may be implemented, as negotiated with the impacted
     landowners.
     Country Club Heights
     Country Club Heights consists of a 14 lot single family residential subdivision
     located west of the intersection of Jackie Court and North Stearns Road, north of
     East F Street. This community is located within Future Specific Plan Area 8, which is
     in the City’s existing and future SOI. The lot sizes range between 1.0 and 1.4 acres.
     This subdivision is fully developed with the exception of one lot. The Future Specific
     Plan designation is applied to those locations intended to accommodate new growth
     during the horizon of the General Plan, and is subject to future comprehensive
     planning through a specific plan and annexation into the City.
     Water
     Domestic water is provided by individual ground water wells. If annexed into the
     City, Oakdale would provide water service to Country Club Heights. Potential points
     of connection to the City’s water system include the supply lines located on the west
     side of the Belsera Subdivision, located just south of this community. Future water
     systems are also anticipated to be constructed for the Trieste subdivision, located
     between the Belsera Subdivision and Highway 108, and may provide alternative
LU-60                   LAND USE AND COMMUNITY FORM

                                              City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
points of connection. Full domestic/fire system development will be required for the
Country Club Heights community in order to meet City standards.
Wastewater
Wastewater is disposed of by individual septic systems. If annexed, the City of
Oakdale would provide wastewater service. Potential points of connection into the
City’s system include the supply lines located on the west side of the Belsera
Subdivision, located just south of this community. Future sewer collection systems
are also anticipated to be constructed for the Trieste subdivision, located between
the Belsera Subdivision and Highway 108, and may provide alternative points of
connection.
Storm Drainage
The storm drainage system for the County Club Heights community consists of drain
inlets connected to dry wells. The subdivision map for this community shows a 7.5
foot wide drainage easement between lots 8 and 9, although no evidence of any
storm drainage facilities exist based upon a field survey.
Dry Utilities
The Country Club Heights community is served by underground electrical and
telephone service, as well as underground natural gas by PG&E. However, there is
no evidence of cable television service. Street lights meeting City standards are
present in the community.
Fire Protection
The Oakdale Rural Fire Protection District currently provides emergency and fire
protection services to the greater Oakdale area (outside of the City limits). Upon
annexation this community would be served by the City of Oakdale Fire
Department. The nearest fire station to this community is Station #5 located at 325
East G Street in the City of Oakdale.
Access
The street network appears to comply with current city and fire standards and it is
not anticipated that additional upgrades would be necessary.
Financing
Future annexation of Country Club Heights would likely occur as part of the Future
Specific Plan Area 8 annexation to be approved by Stanislaus County LAFCO.
After annexation, water, wastewater, and storm drainage infrastructure
improvements will be required, and fire protection service will need to be provided
by the City of Oakdale.
Potential financing mechanisms to improve water and wastewater infrastructure to
City standards include: city impact fees; developer financing; and/or a community
facilities district (CFD). The City of Oakdale has adopted a set of development
impact fees (i.e., Community Facilities Fees) to finance capital improvements. Fees
are collected prior to issuance of a building permit for water, wastewater, storm
drainage, and fire protection. Future updates to the City’s fee program may include
                                    LAND USE & COMMUNITY FORM                       LU-61

     City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
     certain improvements within the Future Specific Plan Area 8 and the Country Club

     fund various facilities and services. The proceeds from a CFD bond sale can be

     Belsera Subdivision

     Future Specific Plan designation is applied to those locations intended to
     accommodate new growth during the horizon of the General Plan, and is subject to
     future comprehensive planning through a specific plan and annexation into the
     City.
     Water
     Domestic water is provided by the City of Oakdale from the Burchell Hill
     Subdivision, located west of the Belsera Subdivision. No upgrades are anticipated
     to be required if this community is annexed into the City.
     Wastewater
     Wastewater collection is currently provided by the City of Oakdale from the Burchell
     Hill Subdivision, located west of the Belsera Subdivision. No upgrades are
     anticipated to be required if this community is annexed into the City.
     Storm Drainage
     The Belsera community owns and maintains a private street network and storm
     drain system. The storm drain system includes a basin. No upgrades are
     anticipated to be required if this community is annexed into the City.
     Dry Utilities
     The site is served by underground electrical and telephone, as well as underground
     natural gas by PG&E. However, there is no evidence of cable television service.
     Fire Protection
     The Oakdale Rural Fire Protection District currently provides emergency and fire
     protection services to the greater Oakdale area (outside of the City limits). Upon
     annexation this community would be served by the City of Oakdale Fire

     East G Street in the City of Oakdale.

Twildo Avenue and Seaman Drive Community
This 42 parcel, single-family residential community is located on Twildo Avenue and
Seaman Drive west of Stearns Road on the east side of Oakdale. South of Highway
108, this community is situated within the City’s existing and future SOI and the
Sierra Pointe Specific Plan (SPSP) area. Both the specific plan and the 2030
General Plan designate this community as Very Low Density Residential, at two to
five dwelling units per acre.
Water
The Twildo Avenue and Seaman Drive community forms Improvement District 22,
which was created to provide domestic water to members of the district by use of a
community owned well and distribution system. The system is operated and
maintained by the Oakdale Irrigation District. The existing water system consists of
8-inch mainlines, fire hydrants, a well, a pump station, and an 8,000 gallon
pneumatic tank. Although the system capacity is sufficient for providing domestic
water needs, it is not capable of providing adequate fire flows to the community.
Fire hydrants have been installed at a frequency that appears to meet City
standards at 300 feet maximum spacing.
If this community were annexed into Oakdale, upgrading of the community water
system to meet city standards would be necessary. This would require a connection
to the City’s water system. Currently, the nearest point of connection available is at
Orsi Road approximately 1,200 feet west of the community. It would be necessary
to acquire easements for construction of a water line through private property as
there are no public rights of way available to accomplish this connection. Future
points of connection may become available as other development in the SPSP east
of the community occurs. Connection to the City water system would provide
sufficient capacity to meet fire flow demands assuming there is adequate capacity
within the City’s system at these points. The existing tanks, wells, and pumps would
require abandonment.
Wastewater
All of the parcels within the community use private septic systems, with no public
sanitary sewer services provided.
                                    LAND USE & COMMUNITY FORM                         LU-63

     City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
     In order to provide City wastewater service to this community, it would be necessary
     to construct approximately 3,500 feet of 8-inch sanitary sewer mainline, 42
     services, 15 manholes and cleanouts as needed. It may be necessary to include
     lifting capabilities for some of the houses in low lying areas. The nearest point of
     connection available is at Orsi Road approximately 1,200 feet west of the
     community. It would be necessary to acquire easements for construction of a
     wastewater line through private property as there are no public rights of way
     available to accomplish this connection. Future points of connection may become
     available as other development in the SPSP east of the community occurs.
     Storm Drainage
     There does not appear to be any storm water collection infrastructure in the
     community, and all drainage appears to be surface runoff. If this community were
     annexed into Oakdale, storm drainage improvements would need to be made to be
     consistent with City standards, which would require curb and gutter to be installed
     along both sides of the streets within the community. Storm drainage would be
     accomplished through an underground storm drain system consisting of 12-18 inch
     mainlines, drywells or retention/percolation systems, and drainage inlets.
     Dry Utilities
     The Twildo Avenue and Seaman Drive community is served by overhead electrical
     and telephone. There does not appear to be cable television, natural gas or
     propane service in the community.
     Fire Protection
     The Oakdale Rural Fire Protection District currently provides emergency and fire
     protection services to the greater Oakdale area (outside of the City limits). Upon
     annexation this community would be served by the City of Oakdale Fire
     Department. The nearest fire station to this community is Station #5 located at 325
     East G Street in the City of Oakdale.
     Access
     Significant improvements would be necessary to provide access by fire apparatus.
     Both Twildo Avenue and Seaman Drive are dead end roads. It would be necessary
     to construct roads that allow fire apparatus full access for turn-around movements
     (cul-de-sac or hammerhead).
     Financing
     As the Twildo Avenue and Seaman Drive community is part of the SPSP, annexation
     of this existing community would occur as part of a larger application to be
     submitted and approved by Stanislaus County LAFCO. A Fiscal Analysis/Public
     Facilities Financing Plan was prepared for the entire specific plan area. The Fiscal
     Analysis assesses impacts of development on the City’s ability to fund services.
     Overall, development of SPSP would be adequately provided public services and
     utilities by the City of Oakdale after annexation. The Public Facilities Financing Plan
     identifies the cost of backbone infrastructure to serve the specific plan area, the
     allocation and ability of properties to support those costs, and the potential funding
LU-64                    LAND USE AND COMMUNITY FORM

                                             City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
and financing mechanisms that can used to construct the infrastructure and facilities
for the SPSP. Based on this Financing Plan, a Financing Strategy was prepared.
Potential financing mechanisms to improve water, wastewater, and storm drainage
infrastructure, and to provide fire protection services, to City standards in this
community include: city impact fees; developer financing; and/or a community
facilities district (CFD). The City of Oakdale has adopted a set of development
impact fees (i.e., Community Facilities Fees) to finance capital improvements. Fees
are collected prior to issuance of a building permit for water, wastewater, storm
drainage, and fire protection. Future updates to the City’s fee program may include
certain improvements within the SPSP and the Twildo Avenue and Seaman Drive
community. Developer financing is another mechanism that may be required to
fund backbone infrastructure and facilities for shortfall financing and for in-tract
subdivision improvements. A CFD may also be established to help pay for
improvements. The 1982 Mello-Roos Community Facilities Act enables cities to
establish a CFD to fund various facilities and services. The proceeds from a CFD
bond sale can be used for direct funding of improvements. Other fee, assessment or
alternative funding mechanisms focused upon improvements associated with the
Twildo Avenue and Seaman Drive community may be implemented, as negotiated
with the impacted landowners.
Tioga Road (Improvement District 41)
This 38 parcel community is located on Tioga Road east of Crane Road on the west
side of Oakdale. It is located within the City’s existing and future SOI, and is
designated as Rural Estate by the 2030 General Plan with a density of two or less
dwelling units per acre. This community forms Improvement District 41, which was
created to provide domestic water to members of the district by use of a community
owned well and distribution system.
Water
The water system serving the Tioga Road community is operated and maintained by
the Oakdale Irrigation District. The system consists of 8-inch mainlines, two wells
and pump stations, and two pneumatic tanks. The tanks are 5,000 gallons and
2,500 gallons each. The tank sizes and well capacities are not sufficient to provide
adequate fire flows to the community; however, fire hydrants have been installed at
various locations within the community.
If the Tioga Road community is annexed into Oakdale, improvements would need
to be made in order to meet city standards. It would be necessary to connect the
Improvement District’s system to the City’s water system at each end of Tioga Road.
This would provide a looped system and is anticipated to provide adequate
capacity to meet fire flow demands assuming there is adequate capacity within the
City’s system at these points. The existing tanks, wells, and pumps would be
abandoned. It will also be necessary to install additional fire hydrants every 300
feet to provide adequate fire flows to serve the community.
                                    LAND USE & COMMUNITY FORM                        LU-65

     City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
     Wastewater
     All of the parcels within the district use private septic systems, with no public
     sanitary sewer services provided.
     In order to provide wastewater service to this community if annexation occurs, it will
     be necessary to construct approximately 3,700 feet of 8-inch sanitary sewer
     mainline, 38 services, 10 manholes and cleanouts as needed. It may be necessary
     to include lifting capabilities for some of the houses in low lying areas. Crane Road
     does not contain any sanitary sewer mainlines, so it would be necessary to connect
     to the City system at the eastern side of the community.
     Storm Drainage
     Approximately 2,700 feet of curb and gutter was constructed as part of the
     Vineyards Subdivision, located adjacent and to the north of the Tioga Road
     community. The curb and gutter collects runoff for most of the eastern part of the
     community and discharges it into French drains under the park basins within the
     Vineyards Subdivision. A small amount of runoff is collected at the intersection of
     Tioga Road and Crane Road and discharges into the City’s Crane Road mainline,
     which flows to the Stanislaus River outfall.
     As noted above, approximately 2,700 feet of curb and gutter currently exists on the
     north side of Tioga Road. Storm drainage improvements would need to be made to
     meet City standards, which would require curb and gutter to be installed along the
     remainder of Tioga Road. It is anticipated that drainage inlets would be installed at
     a maximum frequency of 400 feet. Further investigation will be required to confirm
     that the existing storm drain system within the Vineyards Subdivision was designed
     to accommodate this area. Underground piping would be required for cross
     connections to the existing system along the north side of Tioga Road. Additional
     mainline storm drain pipelines may be required at each end of the community.
     Dry Utilities
     The Tiago Road community is served by overhead electric, telephone and cable as
     well as underground natural gas. Some of the overhead utilities are in Tioga Road
     and some are located in the alley to the south of the community. There is no street
     lighting along Tioga Road.
     Fire Protection
     The Oakdale Rural Fire Protection District currently provides emergency and fire
     protection services to the greater Oakdale area (outside of the City limits). Upon
     annexation this community would be served by the City of Oakdale Fire
     Department. The nearest fire station to this community is Station #4 located at 450
     South Willowood Drive in the City of Oakdale.
     Access
     Tioga Road is a 20 feet wide dead end street. There is also an existing gravel alley
     running along the back of the dwellings on the south side of the community.
     Significant improvements will be necessary to provide access by fire apparatus. For
     a dead end road of this length, special approval will be required. It may be
LU-66                   LAND USE AND COMMUNITY FORM

                                             City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
necessary to provide a minimum of 26 feet paved width with turn-around (cul-de-
sac or hammerhead) for Tioga Road. Alley access may not be a viable form of
access for fire apparatus. An emergency access roadway exists on Tioga Road for
access to the Vineyard Subdivision.
Financing
If the Tioga Road community is annexed into the City of Oakdale, water,
wastewater, and storm drainage infrastructure improvements will be required, and
fire protection service will need to be provided by the City of Oakdale.
Potential financing mechanisms to improve water, wastewater, and storm drain
infrastructure to City standards in this community, as well as roadway improvements
for emergency access include: city impact fees; and/or community facilities district
(CFD). The City of Oakdale has adopted a set of development impact fees (i.e.,
Community Facilities Fees) to finance capital improvements. Fees are collected prior
to issuance of a building permit for water, wastewater, storm drainage, streets and
fire protection. Future updates to the City’s fee program may include certain
improvements within this community. A CFD may also be established to help pay
for improvements. The 1982 Mello-Roos Community Facilities Act enables cities to
establish a CFD to fund various facilities and services. The proceeds from a CFD
bond sale can be used for direct funding of improvements. Other fee, assessment or
alternative funding mechanisms focused upon improvements associated with the
Tioga Road community may be implemented, as negotiated with the impacted
landowners.
                                    LAND USE & COMMUNITY FORM                       LU-67


OAKDALE 2030 GENERAL PLAN
ECONOMY VITALITY

                                               City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
                     Economic Vitality
The Economic Vitality Element presents a set of goals and policies to enhance the
economic well-being and sustainability of the City. Guidance is provided to achieve
the community’s vision to attract, grow and retain a stable and diverse range of
businesses. It is the overall intent of this element to further elevate Oakdale as a
great place to live, work and do business.
    Our economic vitality vision includes…                                           CONTENTS
       A fiscally responsible City                                                  Context
       High quality financially sustainable growth                                  Policy Framework
       A local business friendly environment                                        EV-1    Fiscal Management
       A diversity of retail, restaurants, services and other revenue               EV-2    Economic Development Tools
        generating uses                                                              EV-3    Business Retention and
       A broad range of employment opportunities                                            Attraction
       Land use and infrastructure capacity attractive to business                  EV-4    Land Supply and
        development                                                                          Infrastructure
       A well trained and educated workforce                                        EV-5    Collaboration/Workforce
                                                                                             Development
                                                 ECONOMIC VITALITY                  EV-1

    City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
      Context
    The City of Oakdale has identified economic growth as a key priority. It is the
    community’s collective vision to not only retain and grow its existing businesses, but
    to attract new revenue and job generating uses. Such economic growth can help to
    satisfy unmet local retail and service demands, capture regional and pass through
    sales tax, stimulate new and diverse job growth, increase disposable household
    income, and generate revenue to support local services. While the City cannot
    control larger market forces and cycles, it can strategically position itself to
    maximize potential opportunities, foster a climate attractive to businesses, and
    ensure responsible and efficient use of available fiscal resources.
    Oakdale’s economy is centered around manufacturing goods and food products,
    retail sales, and providing services to residents and visitors. During the 1990’s and
    early 2000’s, the City experienced rapid residential development that was not
    matched by corresponding job growth. In recent years the number of jobs in the
    City has declined, the most notable example being the closing of the Hershey
    Chocolate Factory in 2008. The combination of recent job losses and rapid
    residential construction has led to a reduction in Oakdale’s jobs-housing ratio to
    less than one job per household. At this level, some Oakdale residents must
    commute outside the City for employment. In addition, the City must provide
    services to residents without as much revenue support from employment-related
    land uses.
EV-2                  ECONOMIC VITALITY

                                               City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
Industrial activity is a major component of the City’s economy. Manufacturing and
food processing, packaging, and shipping companies are some of Oakdale’s
largest employers. The City is an attractive location for these businesses because of
its proximity to productive farmlands and nearby rail lines that transport raw
materials and agricultural products for processing and wholesale distribution.
Opportunities exist for the City to build upon and diversify its existing job base,
leverage its increasingly educated workforce, and capitalize on access to the future
North County Corridor and the Oakdale Municipal Airport. Positioning for future
job growth industries will require a clear set of economic development priorities and
strategies.
Oakdale’s retail expenditure patterns give the City one of the highest rates of
taxable sales per capita in Stanislaus County, but sales growth has increased less
than some cities, suggesting potential for expanded City retail offerings. For future
retail positioning, the City will need to decrease leakage in regional- and
community-level retail sales to other areas, particularly Riverbank and Modesto.
Expansion of Oakdale’s retail offerings is most likely in highway commercial uses
(pass through trips to Yosemite and the Sierras) and in additional regional retail
outlets such as auto dealers, apparel, furniture, restaurants, and entertainment and
leisure outlets, as discussed in the Oakdale Retail Analysis prepared by MTN Retail
Advisors in May 2012. Oakdale has the potential to increase its capture of regional
and highway commercial spending by identifying adequate sites meeting the traffic,
access, and visibility requirements of these types of retailers. Increased retail
spending generates increased sales tax revenue which can help fund City services.
A former economic development tool and funding source for revitalization of
Oakdale’s downtown and portions of its commercial corridors was the City’s
redevelopment program, which was administered by the Oakdale Community
Redevelopment Agency (City Council). However, all redevelopment agencies in
California were dissolved effective February 1, 2012, and the City now serves as
the Successor Agency to the Oakdale Community Redevelopment Agency. As the
Successor Agency, Oakdale is responsible for overseeing the wind down of the
City’s redevelopment activities. An Oversight Board consisting of seven regionally
appointed representatives reviews and approves actions taken by the Successor
Agency. The State Department of Finance may review the actions of the Oversight
Board. While alternatives to redevelopment have been discussed, at the time of this
General Plan’s adoption, no comparable programs had been initiated. The
elimination of redevelopment further challenges funding sources available to the
City to support revitalization and economic development.
The General Plan includes policies to help guide the long-term economic growth of
the City in a manner that capitalizes on potential market opportunities. Emphasis is
placed on the need to develop and implement a comprehensive economic
development strategy that supports business attraction, growth and retention. In
addition, guidance is provided to further the management and delivery of
essential City services in a financially sound and cost-effective manner.

OAKDALE 2030 GENERAL PLAN
MOBILITY

                                              City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
                                                       Mobility
The Mobility Element establishes policies and programs that support a balanced,
multi-modal transportation network accommodating motor vehicles, bicyclists,
pedestrians, public transit, rail and air travel. The intent is to efficiently serve the
growth anticipated by the Land Use Element, and to ensure that improvements CONTENTS
are consistent with community character.                                                 Context
    Our mobility vision includes…                                                        Policy Framework
       A balanced and interconnected mobility system                                    M-1     Multimodal Transportation
                                                                                                 Choices
       Safe and efficient movement of people and goods
                                                                                         M-2     Vehicular Travel
       Expanded multi-modal transportation options
                                                                                         M-3     Bicyclists, Pedestrians &
       Reduced vehicle trips
                                                                                                 Equestrians
       Adequate capital facilities programs
                                                                                         M-4     Public Transit
       Interagency Coordination

                                                                                         M-6     Aviation
                                                                                         M-7     Funding & Regional
                                                                                                 Coordination
                                                           MOBILITY                     M-1

   City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
     Context
   The Mobility Element guides the improvement, operation and maintenance of the
   City’s transportation network. The ability to efficiently, safely and conveniently
   travel within and through Oakdale is critical to the City’s economic growth,
   health, character and sustainability. The term “mobility” acknowledges that the
   transportation needs of the community are defined by a range of travel options.
   The attractiveness of certain travel options is directly influenced by the type and
   pattern of land uses as well as the availability of transportation facilities and
   services. While it is acknowledged that the automobile is and will continue to be
   the primary mode of travel, the General Plan Land Use and Mobility elements
   combine to promote increased accessibility to and use of alternative
   transportation modes.
   The City’s mobility system includes the following components:
      Roadways and Highways – The roadway network in Oakdale is
       characterized by high traffic volumes along the two major arterials through
       the City, F Street and Yosemite Avenue, with relatively low volumes on the
       remainder of the City’s streets. F Street and the northern portion of Yosemite
       Avenue are designated as state highways (SR 108 and SR 120) and as such
       are under Caltrans jurisdiction. These roadways serve much of the City’s
       cross-town and commute travel, and carry significant volumes of traffic
       between the San Joaquin Valley and Sierra Nevada destinations including
       Yosemite. In addition, Yosemite Avenue provides Oakdale’s only bridge
       over the Stanislaus River. The City has no ability to unilaterally make
       improvements to state highways.
M-2                 MOBILITY

                                          City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
  The North County Corridor (NCC) is a planned state freeway/
  expressway connecting SR 99 north of Modesto to SR 120 east of
  Oakdale. This facility is intended to serve as a bypass to F Street and will
  become SR 108, reducing traffic congestion within the cities of Modesto,
  Riverbank and Oakdale. At the time of this General Plan approval, a
  final alignment for the NCC has not been adopted. Alignments being
  studied lie south and east of the City, with connections to Crane Road,
  Yosemite Avenue, and Stearns Road. Once the NCC is constructed, SR
  108 (F Street west of Yosemite Avenue) would be relinquished to the City.
  In anticipation of relinquishment, the City is a participant with the cities of
  Modesto and Riverbank in the SR 108 Relinquishment and Reinvestment
  Plan. This plan is intended to identify strategies to stimulate and revitalize
  the SR 108 corridor once relinquished to local control.
  The City of Oakdale’s streets and highways are organized in a hierarchy
  according to their functional classification. This hierarchy recognizes the
  type, volume and character of intended traffic service. The City’s
  roadway classifications are summarized on Table M-1. Oakdale’s
  planned roadway system (arterials and collectors) is illustrated on the
  Circulation Diagram, Figure M-1. In addition to new roadways planned
  as part of the City’s specific plans, existing City roadways will need to be
  modified to implement the Circulation Diagram, which is summarized on
  Table M-2. Planned roadways are based upon buildout of the General
  Plan and assume construction of the NCC.
  The Circulation Diagram and General Plan policies reflect the City’s
  acceptance of reduced roadway levels-of-service where required
  improvements are determined infeasible or would conflict with other City
  goals. As an example, roadway widenings to add lanes within
  Downtown would adversely impact existing structures/businesses and
  impede pedestrian/bicycle circulation. The General Plan recognizes that
  in some instances, accepting added congestion along select roadways is
  less disruptive to community character than the roadway improvements
  that would be required to increase capacity. Three roadway segments
  were identified through the environmental review analysis that would
  operate below the City’s base Level of Service standard and where
  roadway improvements could not be feasibly implemented (see Policy M-
  2.6). Public roads and streetscapes are to present pleasant settings scaled
  to pedestrians with strong relationships to adjacent buildings.
 Bicycle & Pedestrian System – A safe, efficient, and well-connected
  bicycle and pedestrian system provides options to automobile travel.
  Biking and walking offer both an enjoyable activity, as well physical
  exercise which has added health benefits. A majority of roadways in the
  City provide sidewalks and crossings for pedestrians. In 2006, the City
  adopted a Bicycle and Pedestrian Master Plan. That plan outlines the
  goals and policies associated with implementing the City’s desire to
  provide residents and visitors with a connected bikeway and trail system
                                                       MOBILITY                   M-3

   City of Oakdale 2030 General Plan
       that accommodates both commute and recreational trips throughout the
       City. This effort was coordinated with the Stanislaus County Non-
       Motorized Transportation Plan. The City’s existing and planned bicycle
       facilities are illustrated on Figure M-2. At the time of General Plan
       adoption there were limited existing connections along the major arterials
       through Oakdale for bicyclists, and no existing facilities along the
       Stanislaus River corridor.
      Public Transit – Transit also offers an alternative transportation mode.
       Oakdale is served by two transit providers, Stanislaus Regional Transit
       (StaRT) and Riverbank-Oakdale Transit Authority (ROTA). StaRT offers fixed-
       route service, and ROTA offers Dial-A-Ride services. Given relatively low
       demand and continued funding challenges, opportunities to use transit in
       Oakdale are limited and ridership relatively low. The General Plan calls for
       continued coordination with local transit providers to maintain and expand
       timely, affordable and responsive transit services.
      Truck Routes and Rail – The efficient movement of goods is critical to the
       economy. There are a number of designated truck routes in the City,
       including SR 108 and SR 120. In addition, the City is served by four rail
       lines: the Burlington Northern and Santa Fe (BNSF) Railroad, the Union
       Pacific (UP) Railroad, the Oakdale Traction Corporation (OTC), and the
       Sierra Railroad. The railroad tracks through the City primarily serve the
       movement of commerce, however, the Sierra Railroad tracks have passenger
       trains that provide various services. Truck routes and rail lines through
       Oakdale are illustrated on Figure M-3.
      Airport - The Oakdale Municipal Airport is located southeast of the City.
       The airfield was originally constructed in 1945, and has been owned and
       operated by the City of Oakdale since 1960. The 117 acre airfield contains
       a single 3,020 foot runway which serves single and twin engine aircraft, as
       well as business jets. There are also aviation related businesses located at the
       field. At the time of General Plan adoption, The City was in discussions to
       update the Oakdale Municipal Airport Master Plan, which could change the
       planning vision of the field. Several areas of study, including the
       expandability of the airport, adjacent development opportunities, and the
       creation of industrial parks or an airpark community, may be studied.
